href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Culture_of_England&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">This article is about English folk music. It is not to be confused with <a href="/wiki/English_people" title="English people">ethnic group from England</a>.</div>\n<p>The <b>folk music of England</b> is tradition-based music, which has existed since the later <a href="/wiki/Medieval" class="mw-redirect" title="Medieval">medieval</a> period. It is often contrasted with courtly, <a href="/wiki/Classical_music" title="Classical music">classical</a> and later commercial music. Folk music has been preserved and transmitted orally, through print and later through recordings. The term is used to refer to English <a href="/wiki/Traditional_music" class="mw-redirect" title="Traditional music">traditional music</a> and music composed, or delivered, in a traditional style. English folk music has produced or contributed to several important musical genres, including <a href="/wiki/Sea_shanties" class="mw-redirect" title="Sea shanties">sea shanties</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jigs" class="mw-redirect" title="Jigs">jigs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hornpipe" title="Hornpipe">hornpipes</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dance_music" title="Dance music">dance music</a>, such as that used for <a href="/wiki/Morris_dancing" class="mw-redirect" title="Morris dancing">Morris dancing</a>. It can be seen as having distinct regional and local variations in content and style, particularly in areas more removed from the cultural and political centres of the English state, as in <a href="/wiki/Northumbria" class="mw-redirect" title="Northumbria">Northumbria</a>, or the <a href="/wiki/West_Country" title="West Country">West Country</a>. Cultural interchange and processes of migration mean that English folk music, although in many ways distinctive, has particularly interacted with the music of <a href="/wiki/Music_of_Scotland" title="Music of Scotland">Scotland</a>. It has also interacted with other musical traditions, particularly classical and <a href="/wiki/Rock_music" title="Rock music">rock music</a>, influencing musical styles and producing <a href="/wiki/Fusion_(music)" class="mw-redirect" title="Fusion (music)">musical fusions</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/British_folk_rock" title="British folk rock">British folk rock</a>, <a href="/wiki/Folk_punk" title="Folk punk">folk punk</a> and <a href="/wiki/Folk_metal" title="Folk metal">folk metal</a>. There remains a flourishing <a href="/wiki/Sub-culture" class="mw-redirect" title="Sub-culture">sub-culture</a> of English folk music, which continues to influence other genres and occasionally gains mainstream attention.\n</p>\n<div id="toc" class="toc"><input type="checkbox" role="button" id="toctogglecheckbox" class="toctogglecheckbox" style="display:none" /><div class="toctitle" lang="en" 18th century</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-4"><a href="#Early_19th_century"><span class="tocnumber">1.3</span> <span class="toctext">Early 19th century</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-5"><a href="#Folk_revivals_1890\xe2\x80\x931969"><span class="tocnumber">1.4</span> <span class="toctext">Folk revivals 1890\xe2\x80\x931969</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-6"><a href="#Progressive_folk"><span class="tocnumber">1.5</span> <span class="toctext">Progressive folk</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-7"><a href="#British_folk_rock"><span class="tocnumber">1.6</span> <span class="toctext">British folk rock</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-8"><a href="#Folk_punk"><span class="tocnumber">1.7</span> <span class="toctext">Folk punk</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-9"><a href="#Folk_metal"><span class="tocnumber">1.8</span> <span class="toctext">Folk metal</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-10"><a href="#Traditional_folk_resurgence_1990-present"><span class="tocnumber">1.9</span> <span class="toctext">Traditional folk resurgence 1990-present</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-11"><a href="#Folk_clubs"><span class="tocnumber">2</span> <span class="toctext">Folk clubs</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-12"><a href="#Folk_music_and_the_radio"><span class="tocnumber">3</span> <span class="toctext">Folk music and the radio</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-13"><a href="#Folk_festivals"><span class="tocnumber">4</span> <span class="toctext">Folk festivals</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-14"><a href="#Forms_of_folk_music"><span class="tocnumber">5</span> <span class="toctext">Forms of folk music</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-15"><a href="#Ballads"><span class="tocnumber">5.1</span> <span class="toctext">Ballads</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-16"><a href="#Carols"><span class="tocnumber">5.2</span> <span class="toctext">Carols</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-17"><a href="#Children&#39;s_songs"><span class="tocnumber">5.3</span> <span class="toctext">Children\'s songs</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-18"><a href="#Erotic_folk_songs"><span class="tocnumber">5.4</span> <span class="toctext">Erotic folk songs</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-19"><a href="#Hornpipes"><span class="tocnumber">5.5</span> <span class="toctext">Hornpipes</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-20"><a href="#Jigs"><span class="tocnumber">5.6</span> <span class="toctext">Jigs</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-21"><a href="#Morris_dance"><span class="tocnumber">5.7</span> <span class="toctext">Morris dance</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-22"><a href="#Protest_songs"><span class="tocnumber">5.8</span> <span class="toctext">Protest songs</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-23"><a href="#Sea_shanties"><span class="tocnumber">5.9</span> <span class="toctext">Sea shanties</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-24"><a href="#War_songs"><span class="tocnumber">5.10</span> <span class="toctext">War songs</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-25"><a href="#Work_songs"><span class="tocnumber">5.11</span> <span class="toctext">Work songs</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-26"><a href="#Regional_traditions"><span class="tocnumber">6</span> <span class="toctext">Regional traditions</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-27"><a href="#East_Anglia"><span class="tocnumber">6.1</span> <span class="toctext">East Anglia</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-28"><a href="#The_Midlands"><span class="tocnumber">6.2</span> <span class="toctext">The Midlands</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-29"><a href="#The_North_West"><span class="tocnumber">6.3</span> <span class="toctext">The North West</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-30"><a href="#Northumbria"><span class="tocnumber">6.4</span> <span class="toctext">Northumbria</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-31"><a href="#The_South_East"><span class="tocnumber">6.5</span> <span class="toctext">The South East</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-32"><a href="#London"><span class="tocnumber">6.5.1</span> <span class="toctext">London</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-33"><a href="#Sussex"><span class="tocnumber">6.5.2</span> <span class="toctext">Sussex</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-34"><a href="#The_West_Country"><span class="tocnumber">6.6</span> <span class="toctext">The West Country</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-35"><a href="#Cornwall"><span class="tocnumber">6.6.1</span> <span class="toctext">Cornwall</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-36"><a href="#The_rest_of_the_West_Country"><span class="tocnumber">6.6.2</span> <span class="toctext">The rest of the West Country</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-37"><a href="#Yorkshire"><span class="tocnumber">6.7</span> <span class="toctext">Yorkshire</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-38"><a href="#Notes"><span class="tocnumber">7</span> <span class="toctext">Notes</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-39"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">8</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Folk_music_of_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Origins">Origins</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Folk_music_of_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Origins">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Pastime.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/Pastime.jpg/220px-Pastime.jpg" width="220" height="168" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/Pastime.jpg/330px-Pastime.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/Pastime.jpg/440px-Pastime.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1766" data-file-height="1346" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Pastime.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Original score of <i>Pastime with Good Company</i> (c. 1513), held in the <a href="/wiki/British_Library" title="British Library">British Library</a>, <a href="/wiki/London" title="London">London</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p>In the strictest sense, English folk music has existed since the arrival of the <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Saxon" class="mw-redirect" title="Anglo-Saxon">Anglo-Saxon</a> people in Britain after 400 BCE. The <a href="/wiki/Venerable_Bede" class="mw-redirect" title="Venerable Bede">Venerable Bede</a>\'s story of the cattleman and later ecclesiastical musician <a href="/wiki/C%C3%A6dmon" title="C\xc3\xa6dmon">C\xc3\xa6dmon</a> indicates that in the early medieval period it was normal at feasts to pass around the harp and sing \'vain and idle songs\'.<sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> Since this type of music was rarely notated, we have little knowledge of its form or content.<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> Some later tunes, like those used for <a href="/wiki/Morris_dance" title="Morris dance">Morris dance</a>, may have their origins in this period, but it is impossible to be certain of these relationships.<sup id="cite_ref-Forrest1999_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Forrest1999-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> We know from a reference in <a href="/wiki/William_Langland" title="William Langland">William Langland</a>\'s <a href="/wiki/Piers_Plowman" title="Piers Plowman">Piers Plowman</a>, that ballads about <a href="/wiki/Robin_Hood" title="Robin Hood">Robin Hood</a> were being sung from at least by the late 14th century and the oldest detailed material we have is <a href="/wiki/Wynkyn_de_Worde" title="Wynkyn de Worde">Wynkyn de Worde</a>\'s collection of Robin Hood ballads printed about 1495.<sup id="cite_ref-Sweers2005pp45-9_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sweers2005pp45-9-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="16th_century_to_the_18th_century">16th century to the 18th century</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Folk_music_of_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: 16th century to the 18th century">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>While there was distinct court music, members of the social elite into the 16th century also seem to have enjoyed, and even to have contributed to the music of the people, as <a href="/wiki/Henry_VIII_of_England" title="Henry VIII of England">Henry VIII</a> perhaps did with the tavern song "<a href="/wiki/Pastime_with_Good_Company" title="Pastime with Good Company">Pastime with Good Company</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Peter_Burke_(historian)" title="Peter Burke (historian)">Peter Burke</a> argued that late medieval social elites had their own culture, but were culturally \xe2\x80\x98amphibious\', able to participate in and affect popular traditions.<sup id="cite_ref-Burke1978_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Burke1978-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><div class="mediaContainer" style="width:220px"><audio id="mwe_player_0" controls="" preload="none" style="width:220px" class="kskin" data-durationhint="88.29387755102" data-startoffset="0" data-mwtitle="Henry_VIII_pastime_with_good_company_performed_by_the_dwsChorale.ogg" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons"><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/b/b1/Henry_VIII_pastime_with_good_company_performed_by_the_dwsChorale.ogg" type="audio/ogg; codecs=&quot;vorbis&quot;" data-title="Original Ogg file (135 kbps)" data-shorttitle="Ogg source" data-width="0" data-height="0" data-bandwidth="135094" /><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/b/b1/Henry_VIII_pastime_with_good_company_performed_by_the_dwsChorale.ogg/Henry_VIII_pastime_with_good_company_performed_by_the_dwsChorale.ogg.mp3" type="audio/mpeg" data-title="MP3" data-shorttitle="MP3" data-transcodekey="mp3" data-width="0" data-height="0" data-bandwidth="211904" /></audio></div>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Henry_VIII_pastime_with_good_company_performed_by_the_dwsChorale.ogg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Pastime with Good Company.</div></div></div>\n<p>In the 16th century the changes in the wealth and culture of the upper social orders caused tastes in music to diverge.<sup id="cite_ref-Burke1978_6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Burke1978-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> There was an internationalisation of courtly music in terms of both instruments, such as the <a href="/wiki/Lute" title="Lute">lute</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hammered_dulcimer" title="Hammered dulcimer">dulcimer</a> and early forms of the <a href="/wiki/Harpsichord" title="Harpsichord">harpsichord</a>, and in form with the development of <a href="/wiki/Madrigal_(music)" class="mw-redirect" title="Madrigal (music)">madrigals</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pavane" title="Pavane">pavanes</a> and <a href="/wiki/Galliard" title="Galliard">galliards</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> For other social orders, instruments like the pipe, <a href="/wiki/Tabor_(instrument)" title="Tabor (instrument)">tabor</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bagpipe" class="mw-redirect" title="Bagpipe">bagpipe</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shawm" title="Shawm">shawm</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hurdy-gurdy" title="Hurdy-gurdy">hurdy-gurdy</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Crumhorn" title="Crumhorn">crumhorn</a> accompanied traditional music and community dance.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> The fiddle, well established in England by the 1660s, was unusual in being a key element in both the art music that developed in the <a href="/wiki/Baroque_music" title="Baroque music">baroque</a>, and in popular song and dance.<sup id="cite_ref-Ling1997_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ling1997-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<table role="presentation" class="mbox-small" style="background-color:#f9f9f9;border:1px solid #aaa;color:#000;">\n<tbody><tr>\n<td class="mbox-image"><div class="center"><div class="floatnone"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/50px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png" width="50" height="50" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/75px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/100px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="160" data-file-height="160" /></div></div></td>\n<td class="mbox-text plainlist" style="line-height:1.1em"><div class="haudio">\n<div style="padding:4px 0"><a href="/wiki/File:Vihuela_Jamaica_Playford.ogg" title="File:Vihuela Jamaica Playford.ogg">Vihuela sample</a></div>\n<div><div class="mediaContainer" style="width:220px"><audio id="mwe_player_1" controls="" preload="none" style="width:220px" class="kskin" data-durationhint="131.09151927438" data-startoffset="0" data-mwtitle="Vihuela_Jamaica_Playford.ogg" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons"><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/4/40/Vihuela_Jamaica_Playford.ogg" type="audio/ogg; codecs=&quot;vorbis&quot;" data-title="Original Ogg file (140 kbps)" data-shorttitle="Ogg source" data-width="0" data-height="0" data-bandwidth="140481" /><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/4/40/Vihuela_Jamaica_Playford.ogg/Vihuela_Jamaica_Playford.ogg.mp3" type="audio/mpeg" data-title="MP3" data-shorttitle="MP3" data-transcodekey="mp3" data-width="0" data-height="0" data-bandwidth="196392" /></audio></div></div>\n<div class="description" style="padding:2px 0 0 0">A <a href="/wiki/Vihuela" title="Vihuela">vihuela</a> playing \'Jamaica\' from Playford\'s 4th edition of <i><a href="/wiki/The_English_Dancing_Master" class="mw-redirect" title="The English Dancing Master">The English Dancing Master</a></i></div></div></td></tr>\n<tr><td colspan="2" class="mbox-text" style="line-height:1.1em"><hr /><span><i>Problems playing this file? See <a href="/wiki/Help:Media" title="Help:Media">media help</a>.</i></span></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>By the mid-17th century, the music of the lower social orders was sufficiently alien to the aristocracy and "middling sort" for a process of rediscovery to be needed in order to understand it, along with other aspects of popular culture such as festivals, folklore and dance.<sup id="cite_ref-Burke1978_6-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Burke1978-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> This led to a number of early collections of printed material, including those published by <a href="/wiki/John_Playford" title="John Playford">John Playford</a> as <i><a href="/wiki/The_English_Dancing_Master" class="mw-redirect" title="The English Dancing Master">The English Dancing Master</a></i> (1651), and the private collections of <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Pepys" title="Samuel Pepys">Samuel Pepys</a> (1633\xe2\x80\x931703) and the <a href="/wiki/Roxburghe_Ballads" title="Roxburghe Ballads">Roxburghe Ballads</a> collected by <a href="/wiki/Robert_Harley,_1st_Earl_of_Oxford_and_Earl_Mortimer" title="Robert Harley, 1st Earl of Oxford and Earl Mortimer">Robert Harley</a>, 1st Earl of Oxford and Mortimer (1661\xe2\x80\x931724).<sup id="cite_ref-Sweers2005pp45-9_4-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sweers2005pp45-9-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In the 18th century there were increasing numbers of collections of what was now beginning to be defined as "folk" music, strongly influenced by the <a href="/wiki/Romantic_movement" class="mw-redirect" title="Romantic movement">Romantic movement</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Thomas_D%27Urfey" class="mw-redirect" title="Thomas D&#39;Urfey">Thomas D\'Urfey</a>\'s <i>Wit and Mirth: or, Pills to Purge Melancholy</i> (1719\xe2\x80\x9320) and Bishop <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Percy_(Bishop_of_Dromore)" class="mw-redirect" title="Thomas Percy (Bishop of Dromore)">Thomas Percy</a>\'s <i>Reliques of Ancient English Poetry</i> (1765).<sup id="cite_ref-Sweers2005pp45-9_4-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sweers2005pp45-9-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> The last of these also contained some oral material and by the end of the 18th century this was becoming increasingly common, with collections including John Ritson\'s, <i>The Bishopric Garland</i> (1784), which paralleled the work of figures like <a href="/wiki/Robert_Burns" title="Robert Burns">Robert Burns</a> and <a href="/wiki/Walter_Scott" title="Walter Scott">Walter Scott</a> in Scotland.<sup id="cite_ref-Sweers2005pp45-9_4-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sweers2005pp45-9-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>It was in this period, too, that English folk music traveled across the Atlantic Ocean and became the foundation for and main ancestor of American traditional music. In the colonies, it mixed with styles of music brought by other immigrant groups to create a host of new genres. For instance, English balladry combined with African banjo playing ultimately produced <a href="/wiki/Bluegrass_music" title="Bluegrass music">bluegrass</a> and <a href="/wiki/Country_music" title="Country music">country music</a>, which evolved, when combined with African-American blues, into <a href="/wiki/Rock_and_roll" title="Rock and roll">rock and roll</a>.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_19th_century">Early 19th century</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Folk_music_of_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Early 19th century">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>With the <a href="/wiki/Industrial_Revolution" title="Industrial Revolution">Industrial Revolution</a> the themes of the music of the labouring classes began to change from rural and agrarian life to include industrial <a href="/wiki/Work_songs" class="mw-redirect" title="Work songs">work songs</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Boyes1993_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Boyes1993-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> Awareness that older kinds of song were being abandoned prompted renewed interest in collecting folk songs during the 1830s and 1840s, including the work of <a href="/wiki/William_Sandys_(antiquarian)" title="William Sandys (antiquarian)">William Sandys</a>\' <i>Christmas Carols Ancient and Modern</i> (1833), William Chappell, <i>A Collection of National English Airs</i> (1838) and <a href="/wiki/Robert_Bell_(writer)" title="Robert Bell (writer)">Robert Bell</a>\'s <i>Ancient Poems, Ballads and Songs of the Peasantry of England</i> (1846).<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Technological change made new instruments available and led to the development of silver and <a href="/wiki/Brass_bands" class="mw-redirect" title="Brass bands">brass bands</a>, particularly in industrial centres in the north.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> The shift to urban centres also began to create new types of music, including from the 1850s the <a href="/wiki/Music_hall" title="Music hall">Music hall</a>, which developed from performances in ale houses into theatres and became the dominant locus of English popular music for over a century.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> This combined with increased literacy and print to allow the creation of new songs that initially built on, but began to differ from traditional music as composers like <a href="/wiki/Lionel_Monckton" title="Lionel Monckton">Lionel Monckton</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sidney_Jones_(composer)" title="Sidney Jones (composer)">Sidney Jones</a> created music that reflected new social circumstances.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span id="Folk_revivals_1890.E2.80.931969"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Folk_revivals_1890\xe2\x80\x931969">Folk revivals 1890\xe2\x80\x931969</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Folk_music_of_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Folk revivals 1890\xe2\x80\x931969">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/British_folk_revival" title="British folk revival">British folk revival</a></div>\n<p>From the late 19th century there were a series of movements that attempted to collect, record, preserve and later to perform, English folk music and dance. These are usually separated into two folk revivals.\n</p><p>The first, in the later 19th and early 20th centuries, involved figures including collectors  <a href="/wiki/Sabine_Baring-Gould" title="Sabine Baring-Gould">Sabine Baring-Gould</a> (1834\xe2\x80\x931924), <a href="/wiki/Frank_Kidson" title="Frank Kidson">Frank Kidson</a> (1855\xe2\x80\x931926), <a href="/wiki/Lucy_Broadwood" title="Lucy Broadwood">Lucy Broadwood</a> (1858\xe2\x80\x931939), and <a href="/wiki/Anne_Gilchrist_(collector)" title="Anne Gilchrist (collector)">Anne Gilchrist</a> (1863\xe2\x80\x931954), centred around the Folk Song Society, founded in 1911.<sup id="cite_ref-Sweers2005pp45-9_4-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sweers2005pp45-9-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Francis_James_Child" title="Francis James Child">Francis James Child</a>\'s (1825\xe2\x80\x9396) eight-volume collection <i><a href="/wiki/The_English_and_Scottish_Popular_Ballads" class="mw-redirect" title="The English and Scottish Popular Ballads">The English and Scottish Popular Ballads</a></i> (1882\xe2\x80\x9392) became the most influential in defining the repertoire of subsequent performers, and <a href="/wiki/Cecil_Sharp" title="Cecil Sharp">Cecil Sharp</a> (1859\xe2\x80\x931924), founder of the English Folk Dance Society, was probably the most important figure in understanding of the nature of folk song.<sup id="cite_ref-Sweers2005pp45-9_4-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sweers2005pp45-9-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> The revival was part of a wider national movement in the period around the <a href="/wiki/First_World_War" class="mw-redirect" title="First World War">First World War</a>, and contributed to the creation of a "national" or "pastoral" school of classical music which incorporated traditional songs or motifs, as can be seen in the compositions of <a href="/wiki/Percy_Grainger" title="Percy Grainger">Percy Grainger</a> (1882\xe2\x80\x931961), <a href="/wiki/Ralph_Vaughan_Williams" title="Ralph Vaughan Williams">Ralph Vaughan Williams</a> (1872\xe2\x80\x931951), <a href="/wiki/George_Butterworth" title="George Butterworth">George Butterworth</a> (1885\xe2\x80\x931916), <a href="/wiki/Gustav_Holst" title="Gustav Holst">Gustav Holst</a> (1874\xe2\x80\x931934) and <a href="/wiki/Frederick_Delius" title="Frederick Delius">Frederick Delius</a> (1862\xe2\x80\x931934).<sup id="cite_ref-Brocken2003_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brocken2003-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> In 1932 the Folk-Song Society and the English Folk Dance Society merged to become the <a href="/wiki/English_Folk_Dance_and_Song_Society" title="English Folk Dance and Song Society">English Folk Dance and Song Society</a> (EFDSS).<sup id="cite_ref-Boyes1993_11-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Boyes1993-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Martin_Carthy.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Martin_Carthy.JPG/170px-Martin_Carthy.JPG" width="170" height="227" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Martin_Carthy.JPG/255px-Martin_Carthy.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Martin_Carthy.JPG/340px-Martin_Carthy.JPG 2x" data-file-width="1536" data-file-height="2048" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Martin_Carthy.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Martin_Carthy" title="Martin Carthy">Martin Carthy</a> performing with <a href="/wiki/The_Imagined_Village" title="The Imagined Village">The Imagined Village</a> at <a href="/wiki/Camp_Bestival" title="Camp Bestival">Camp Bestival</a> - 20 July 2008</div></div></div>\n<p>The second revival gained momentum after the <a href="/wiki/Second_World_War" class="mw-redirect" title="Second World War">Second World War</a>, following on from the <a href="/wiki/American_folk_music_revival" title="American folk music revival">American folk music revival</a> as new forms of media and American commercial music appeared to pose another threat to traditional music.<sup id="cite_ref-Brocken2003_16-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brocken2003-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Connell2003_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Connell2003-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> The key figures were <a href="/wiki/Ewan_MacColl" title="Ewan MacColl">Ewan MacColl</a> and <a href="/wiki/A._L._Lloyd" title="A. L. Lloyd">A. L. Lloyd</a>. The second revival was generally left wing in politics and emphasised the work music of the 19th century and previously neglected forms like erotic folk songs.<sup id="cite_ref-Sweers2005pp45-9_4-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sweers2005pp45-9-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Topic_Records" title="Topic Records">Topic Records</a>, founded in 1939, provided a major source of folk recordings.<sup id="cite_ref-Brocken2003_16-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brocken2003-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> The revival resulted in the foundation of a network of <a href="/wiki/Folk_clubs" class="mw-redirect" title="Folk clubs">folk clubs</a> in major towns, from the 1950s.<sup id="cite_ref-Sweers2005pp32-6_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sweers2005pp32-6-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> Major traditional performers included the <a href="/wiki/Copper_Family" title="Copper Family">Copper Family</a>, <a href="/wiki/The_Watersons" title="The Watersons">The Watersons</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Ian_Campbell_Folk_Group" title="Ian Campbell Folk Group">Ian Campbell Folk Group</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Shirley_Collins" title="Shirley Collins">Shirley Collins</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Broughton1999_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Broughton1999-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> The fusing of various styles of American music with English folk also helped to create a distinctive form of guitar <a href="/wiki/Fingerstyle" class="mw-redirect" title="Fingerstyle">fingerstyle</a> known as \xe2\x80\x98<a href="/wiki/Folk_baroque" title="Folk baroque">folk baroque</a>\xe2\x80\x99, which was pioneered by <a href="/wiki/Davy_Graham" class="mw-redirect" title="Davy Graham">Davy Graham</a>, <a href="/wiki/Martin_Carthy" title="Martin Carthy">Martin Carthy</a>, <a href="/wiki/John_Renbourn" title="John Renbourn">John Renbourn</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bert_Jansch" title="Bert Jansch">Bert Jansch</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Sweers2005pp184-9_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sweers2005pp184-9-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Progressive_folk">Progressive folk</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Folk_music_of_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Progressive folk">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Progressive_folk" title="Progressive folk">Progressive folk</a></div>\n<p>The process of fusion between American musical styles and English folk can also be seen as the origin of British <a href="/wiki/Progressive_folk" title="Progressive folk">progressive folk</a> music, which attempted to elevate folk music through greater musicianship, or compositional and arrangement skills.<sup id="cite_ref-Sweers2005p203_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sweers2005p203-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> Many progressive folk performers continued to retain a traditional element in their music, including Jansch and Renbourn, who with <a href="/wiki/Jacqui_McShee" title="Jacqui McShee">Jacqui McShee</a>, <a href="/wiki/Danny_Thompson" title="Danny Thompson">Danny Thompson</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Terry_Cox" title="Terry Cox">Terry Cox</a>, formed <a href="/wiki/Pentangle" title="Pentangle">Pentangle</a> in 1967.<sup id="cite_ref-Sweers2005p40_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sweers2005p40-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> Others totally abandoned the traditional element and in this area particularly influential were the Scottish artists <a href="/wiki/Donovan" title="Donovan">Donovan</a>, who was most influenced by emerging progressive folk musicians in America like <a href="/wiki/Bob_Dylan" title="Bob Dylan">Bob Dylan</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Incredible_String_Band" class="mw-redirect" title="Incredible String Band">Incredible String Band</a>, who from 1967 incorporated a range of influences including medieval and eastern music into their compositions. Some of this, particularly the Incredible String Band, has been seen as developing into the further subgenre of psych or <a href="/wiki/Psychedelic_folk" title="Psychedelic folk">psychedelic folk</a> and had a considerable impact on <a href="/wiki/Progressive_rock" title="Progressive rock">progressive</a> and <a href="/wiki/Psychedelic_rock" title="Psychedelic rock">psychedelic rock</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>There was a brief flowering of English progressive folk in the late 1960s and early 1970s, with groups like the <a href="/wiki/Third_Ear_Band" title="Third Ear Band">Third Ear Band</a> and <a href="/wiki/Quintessence_(English_band)" title="Quintessence (English band)">Quintessence</a> following the eastern Indian musical and more abstract work by group such as <a href="/wiki/Comus_(band)" title="Comus (band)">Comus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dando_Shaft" title="Dando Shaft">Dando Shaft</a>, <a href="/wiki/Trees_(folk_band)" title="Trees (folk band)">The Trees</a>, <a href="/wiki/Spirogyra_(band)" title="Spirogyra (band)">Spirogyra</a>, <a href="/wiki/Forest" title="Forest">Forest</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Jan_Dukes_De_Grey" class="mw-redirect" title="Jan Dukes De Grey">Jan Dukes De Grey</a>, but commercial success was elusive for these bands and most had broken up or moved in very different directions by about 1973. Perhaps the finest individual work in the genre was from artists early 1970s artists like <a href="/wiki/Nick_Drake" title="Nick Drake">Nick Drake</a> and <a href="/wiki/John_Martyn" title="John Martyn">John Martyn</a>, but these can also be considered the first among the English \xe2\x80\x98folk troubadours\xe2\x80\x99 or \xe2\x80\x98singer-songwriters\xe2\x80\x99, individual performers who remained largely acoustic but who relied mostly on their own individual compositions.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> The most successful of these was <a href="/wiki/Ralph_McTell" title="Ralph McTell">Ralph McTell</a>, whose \xe2\x80\x98<a href="/wiki/Streets_of_London_(song)" title="Streets of London (song)">Streets of London</a>\xe2\x80\x99 reached number 2 in the <a href="/wiki/UK_Single_Charts" class="mw-redirect" title="UK Single Charts">UK Single Charts</a> in 1974, and whose music is clearly folk, but without much reliance on tradition, virtuosity, or much evidence of attempts at fusion with other genres.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="British_folk_rock">British folk rock</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Folk_music_of_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: British folk rock">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/British_folk_rock" title="British folk rock">British folk rock</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Fairport_Convention_-_TopPop_1972_7.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Fairport_Convention_-_TopPop_1972_7.png/220px-Fairport_Convention_-_TopPop_1972_7.png" width="220" height="219" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Fairport_Convention_-_TopPop_1972_7.png/330px-Fairport_Convention_-_TopPop_1972_7.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Fairport_Convention_-_TopPop_1972_7.png/440px-Fairport_Convention_-_TopPop_1972_7.png 2x" data-file-width="500" data-file-height="498" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Fairport_Convention_-_TopPop_1972_7.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Fairport_Convention" title="Fairport Convention">Fairport Convention</a> in a Dutch television show in 1972</div></div></div>\n<p>British folk rock developed in Britain during the mid to late 1960s by the bands <a href="/wiki/Fairport_Convention" title="Fairport Convention">Fairport Convention</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Pentangle" title="Pentangle">Pentangle</a> which built on elements of American <a href="/wiki/Folk_rock" title="Folk rock">folk rock</a>, and on the second <a href="/wiki/British_folk_revival" title="British folk revival">British folk revival</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Brocken2003_16-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brocken2003-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> It uses traditional music, and compositions in a traditional style, played on a combination of rock and traditional instruments.<sup id="cite_ref-Sweers2005pp21-5_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sweers2005pp21-5-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> It was most significant in the 1970s, when it was taken up by groups such as <a href="/wiki/Pentangle" title="Pentangle">Pentangle</a>, <a href="/wiki/Steeleye_Span" title="Steeleye Span">Steeleye Span</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Albion_Band" class="mw-redirect" title="Albion Band">Albion Band</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> It was rapidly adopted and developed in the surrounding Celtic cultures of <a href="/wiki/Brittany" title="Brittany">Brittany</a>, where it was pioneered by <a href="/wiki/Alan_Stivell" title="Alan Stivell">Alan Stivell</a> and bands like <a href="/wiki/Malicorne_(band)" title="Malicorne (band)">Malicorne</a>; in <a href="/wiki/Ireland" title="Ireland">Ireland</a> by groups such as <a href="/wiki/Horslips" title="Horslips">Horslips</a>; and also in <a href="/wiki/Scotland" title="Scotland">Scotland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wales" title="Wales">Wales</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Isle_of_Man" title="Isle of Man">Isle of Man</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cornwall" title="Cornwall">Cornwall</a>, to produce <a href="/wiki/Celtic_rock" title="Celtic rock">Celtic rock</a> and its derivatives.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> It has been influential in those parts of the world with close cultural connections to Britain, such as the USA and Canada and gave rise to the subgenre of <a href="/wiki/Medieval_folk_rock" title="Medieval folk rock">Medieval folk rock</a> and the fusion genres of <a href="/wiki/Folk_punk" title="Folk punk">folk punk</a> and <a href="/wiki/Folk_metal" title="Folk metal">folk metal</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Sweers2005pp240-57_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sweers2005pp240-57-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> By the 1980s the genre was in steep decline in popularity, but has survived and revived in significance as part of a more general folk resurgence since the 1990s.<sup id="cite_ref-Sweers2005pp26-70_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sweers2005pp26-70-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Folk_punk">Folk punk</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Folk_music_of_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Folk punk">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Folk_punk" title="Folk punk">folk punk</a></div>\n<p>In the mid-1980s a new rebirth of English folk began, this time fusing folk with energy and political aggression derived from punk rock. Leaders included <a href="/wiki/The_Pogues" title="The Pogues">The Pogues</a>, <a href="/wiki/The_Men_They_Couldn%27t_Hang" title="The Men They Couldn&#39;t Hang">The Men They Couldn\'t Hang</a>, <a href="/wiki/Oyster_Band" class="mw-redirect" title="Oyster Band">Oyster Band</a> and <a href="/wiki/Billy_Bragg" title="Billy Bragg">Billy Bragg</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Sweers2005pp197-8_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sweers2005pp197-8-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> Folk <a href="/wiki/Dance_music" title="Dance music">dance music</a> also became popular in the 80s, with acts like the English Country Blues Band and Tiger Moth.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> The decade later saw the use of <a href="/wiki/Reggae" title="Reggae">reggae</a> with English folk music by the band <a href="/wiki/Edward_II_(band)" title="Edward II (band)">Edward II &amp; the Red Hot Polkas</a>, especially on their seminal <i>Let\'s Polkasteady</i> from 1987.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Folk_metal">Folk metal</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Folk_music_of_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Folk metal">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Folk_metal" title="Folk metal">folk metal</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:DSCN1809-eliza-carthy_crop_500x700.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/DSCN1809-eliza-carthy_crop_500x700.jpg/170px-DSCN1809-eliza-carthy_crop_500x700.jpg" width="170" height="238" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/DSCN1809-eliza-carthy_crop_500x700.jpg/255px-DSCN1809-eliza-carthy_crop_500x700.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/DSCN1809-eliza-carthy_crop_500x700.jpg/340px-DSCN1809-eliza-carthy_crop_500x700.jpg 2x" data-file-width="500" data-file-height="700" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:DSCN1809-eliza-carthy_crop_500x700.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Eliza Carthy</div></div></div>\n<p>In a process strikingly similar to the origins of British folk rock in the 1960s, the English <a href="/wiki/Thrash_metal" title="Thrash metal">thrash metal</a> band <a href="/wiki/Skyclad_(band)" title="Skyclad (band)">Skyclad</a> added violins from a session musician on several tracks for their 1990 debut album <i><a href="/wiki/The_Wayward_Sons_of_Mother_Earth" title="The Wayward Sons of Mother Earth">The Wayward Sons of Mother Earth</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Rivadavia,_Eduardo_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rivadavia,_Eduardo-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> When this was well received they adopted a full-time fiddle player and moved towards a signature folk and jig style leading them to be credited as the pioneers of folk metal, which has spread to Ireland, the Baltic and Germany.<sup id="cite_ref-Rivadavia,_Eduardo_35-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rivadavia,_Eduardo-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Traditional_folk_resurgence_1990-present">Traditional folk resurgence 1990-present</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Folk_music_of_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Traditional folk resurgence 1990-present">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The peak of traditional English folk, like progressive and electric folk, was the mid- to late-1970s, when, for a time it threatened to break through into the mainstream. By the end of the decade, however, it was in decline.<sup id="cite_ref-Bogdanov2002Britfolk_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bogdanov2002Britfolk-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> The attendance at, and numbers of folk clubs began to decrease, probably as new musical and social trends, including <a href="/wiki/Punk_rock" title="Punk rock">punk rock</a>, <a href="/wiki/New_wave_music" title="New wave music">new wave</a> and <a href="/wiki/Electronic_music" title="Electronic music">electronic music</a> began to dominate.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> Although many acts like Martin Carthy and the Watersons continued to perform successfully, there were very few significant new acts pursuing traditional forms in the 1980s. This began to change with a new generation in the 1990s. The arrival and sometimes mainstream success of acts like <a href="/wiki/Kate_Rusby" title="Kate Rusby">Kate Rusby</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bellowhead" title="Bellowhead">Bellowhead</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nancy_Kerr" title="Nancy Kerr">Nancy Kerr</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kathryn_Tickell" title="Kathryn Tickell">Kathryn Tickell</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jim_Moray" title="Jim Moray">Jim Moray</a>, <a href="/wiki/Spiers_and_Boden" title="Spiers and Boden">Spiers and Boden</a>, <a href="/wiki/Seth_Lakeman" title="Seth Lakeman">Seth Lakeman</a>, <a href="/wiki/Frank_Turner" title="Frank Turner">Frank Turner</a>, <a href="/wiki/Laura_Marling" title="Laura Marling">Laura Marling</a> and <a href="/wiki/Eliza_Carthy" title="Eliza Carthy">Eliza Carthy</a>, all largely concerned with acoustic performance of traditional material, marked a radical turn around in the fortunes of the tradition.<sup id="cite_ref-Broughton1999_20-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Broughton1999-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> This was reflected in the adoption creation of the <a href="/wiki/BBC_Radio_2_Folk_Awards" title="BBC Radio 2 Folk Awards">BBC Radio 2 Folk Awards</a> in 2000, which gave the music a much needed status and focus and the profile of folk music is as high in England today as it has been for over thirty years.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Folk_clubs">Folk clubs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Folk_music_of_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Folk clubs">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Folk_clubs" class="mw-redirect" title="Folk clubs">Folk clubs</a></div>\n<p>Although there were a handful of clubs that allowed space for the performance of traditional folk music by the early 1950s, its major boost came from the short-lived British <a href="/wiki/Skiffle" title="Skiffle">skiffle</a> craze, from about 1956-8.<sup id="cite_ref-Brocken2003_16-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brocken2003-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> New clubs included the \xe2\x80\x98Ballad and Blues\xe2\x80\x99 club in a <a href="/wiki/Public_house" class="mw-redirect" title="Public house">pub</a> in <a href="/wiki/Soho" title="Soho">Soho</a>, co-founded by Ewan MacColl.<sup id="cite_ref-Boyes1993_11-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Boyes1993-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> As the craze subsided from the mid-1950s many of these clubs began to shift towards the performance of English traditional folk material.<sup id="cite_ref-Brocken2003_16-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brocken2003-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> Many became strict \xe2\x80\x98policy clubs\xe2\x80\x99, that pursued a pure and traditional form of music.<sup id="cite_ref-Boyes1993_11-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Boyes1993-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> By the mid-1960s there were probably over 300 in Britain.<sup id="cite_ref-Brocken2003_16-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brocken2003-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> Most clubs were simply a regular gathering, usually in the back or upstairs room of a public house on a weekly basis.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> They were largely a phenomenon of the urbanised middle classes and known for the amateur nature of many performances.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> There were also \xe2\x80\x98residents\xe2\x80\x99, who performed regular short sets of songs.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> Many of these later emerged as major performers in their own right, including <a href="/wiki/A._L._Lloyd" title="A. L. Lloyd">A. L. Lloyd</a>, <a href="/wiki/Martin_Carthy" title="Martin Carthy">Martin Carthy</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Shirley_Collins" title="Shirley Collins">Shirley Collins</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> A later generation of performers used the folk club circuit for highly successful mainstream careers, including <a href="/wiki/Billy_Connolly" title="Billy Connolly">Billy Connolly</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jasper_Carrott" title="Jasper Carrott">Jasper Carrott</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ian_Dury" title="Ian Dury">Ian Dury</a> and <a href="/wiki/Barbara_Dickson" title="Barbara Dickson">Barbara Dickson</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Brocken2003_16-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brocken2003-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> The number of clubs began to decline in the 1980s, in the face of changing musical and social trends. But the decline began to stabilize in the mid-1990s with the resurgence of interest in folk music and there are now over 160 folk clubs in the United Kingdom, including many that can trace their origins back to the 1950s.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Folk_music_and_the_radio">Folk music and the radio</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Folk_music_of_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Folk music and the radio">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>The difficulty of gaining regular appearances on television in England has long meant that radio has remained the major popular medium for increasing awareness of the genre. The EFDSS sponsored the <a href="/wiki/BBC_Home_Service" title="BBC Home Service">BBC Home Service</a> radio program, <i>As I Roved Out</i>, based on field recordings made by <a href="/wiki/Peter_Douglas_Kennedy" title="Peter Douglas Kennedy">Peter Kennedy</a> and <a href="/wiki/S%C3%A9amus_Ennis" title="S\xc3\xa9amus Ennis">S\xc3\xa9amus Ennis</a> from 1952 to 1958, which probably did more than any other single factor to introduce the general population to British folk music in the period.<sup id="cite_ref-Sweersp119_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sweersp119-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> Also important were occasional radio shows, such as Lomax\xe2\x80\x99s <i>Ballads and Blues</i> (1951),<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> MacColl\xe2\x80\x99s <i><a href="/wiki/Radio-ballad" class="mw-redirect" title="Radio-ballad">Radio-ballads</a></i> (1958\xe2\x80\x9364) and <i>The Song Carriers</i> (1968).<sup id="cite_ref-Sweersp119_44-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sweersp119-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/John_Peel" title="John Peel">John Peel</a> frequently included folk music of his <i>Top Gear</i> show on Radio One from 1968, but dropped it when punk arrived in the 1970s.<sup id="cite_ref-Sweersp119_44-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sweersp119-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> The most consistent source of folk music on radio, has been <a href="/wiki/BBC_Radio_2" title="BBC Radio 2">BBC Radio 2</a>. In 1967 "My Kind of folk" was broadcast on Wednesdays. In 1970 "Folk on Friday" began, presented by Jim Lloyd. In 1972 it became "Folk on Sunday".<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> "Folkweave" was presented by <a href="/wiki/Tony_Capstick" title="Tony Capstick">Tony Capstick</a> 1975-8. "Folk on Two" (Wednesdays) began in 1980. In 1998 Jim Lloyd retired from the programme and was replaced by <a href="/wiki/Mike_Harding" title="Mike Harding">Mike Harding</a>. In 2007 it was renamed "The Mike Harding Folk Show". In October 2012 it was announced that Mike Harding would be leaving the programme to be replaced by <a href="/wiki/Mark_Radcliffe_(radio_broadcaster)" title="Mark Radcliffe (radio broadcaster)">Mark Radcliffe</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Ian_A._Anderson" title="Ian A. Anderson">Ian A. Anderson</a>, editor of "fRoots", also presented the occasional series for Radio Two. He hosted a World music programme on "Jazz FM" and then spent 10 years broadcasting on the BBC <a href="/wiki/World_Service" class="mw-redirect" title="World Service">World Service</a>. He currently hosts "fRoots Radio" on the web.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> For over twenty years, until 2006, Charlie Gillett presented World music on BBC London.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Folk_festivals">Folk festivals</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Folk_music_of_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Folk festivals">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Folk_festival" class="mw-redirect" title="Folk festival">folk festival</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Cambridge2008.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Cambridge2008.jpg/220px-Cambridge2008.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Cambridge2008.jpg/330px-Cambridge2008.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Cambridge2008.jpg/440px-Cambridge2008.jpg 2x" data-file-width="500" data-file-height="333" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Cambridge2008.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Cambridge Folk Festival 2008</div></div></div>\n<p>Folk festivals began to be organised by the EFDSS from about 1950, usually as local or regional event with an emphasis on dance, like the <a href="/wiki/Sidmouth_Festival" class="mw-redirect" title="Sidmouth Festival">Sidmouth Festival</a> (from 1955) and the Keele Festival (1965), which was abandoned in 1981 but reinstituted three years later as the <a href="/w/index.php?title=National_Folk_Festival_(UK)&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="National Folk Festival (UK) (page does not exist)">National Folk Festival</a>. The EFDSS gave up its organizing role in these festivals in the 1980s and most are locally run and financed.<sup id="cite_ref-Sweers2005pp116-7_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sweers2005pp116-7-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> One of the largest and most prestigious English folk festivals at <a href="/wiki/Cambridge_folk_festival" class="mw-redirect" title="Cambridge folk festival">Cambridge</a> was founded in 1965 and attracts about 10,000 people.<sup id="cite_ref-Sweers2005pp116-7_49-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sweers2005pp116-7-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> Probably the largest is <a href="/wiki/Fairport%27s_Cropredy_Convention" title="Fairport&#39;s Cropredy Convention">Fairport\'s Cropredy Convention</a>, which since 1979 has provided a venue for folk, British folk rock, and rock artists; it now attracts up to 20,000 people a year as well as performances for Fairport Convention and their friends.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> Like rock festivals, folk festivals have begun to multiply since the 1990s and there are over a hundred folk festivals or varying sizes held in England every year.<sup id="cite_ref-folkandroots.co.uk_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-folkandroots.co.uk-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Forms_of_folk_music">Forms of folk music</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Folk_music_of_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Forms of folk music">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ballads">Ballads</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Folk_music_of_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Ballads">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Ballads" class="mw-redirect" title="Ballads">Ballads</a></div>\n<p>A ballad is a form of verse, often a narrative story and set to music. Many ballads were written and sold as single sheet <a href="/wiki/Broadside_(music)" class="mw-redirect" title="Broadside (music)">broadsides</a>. They are usually narrative in structure and make considerable use of repetition.<sup id="cite_ref-Houseman1969_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Houseman1969-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> The traditional ballad has been seen as originating with the wandering <a href="/wiki/Minstrels" class="mw-redirect" title="Minstrels">minstrels</a> of late medieval Europe.<sup id="cite_ref-Houseman1969_52-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Houseman1969-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> There have been many different and contradictory attempts to classify traditional ballads by theme, but commonly identified types are religious, supernatural, tragic, love, historic, legends and humour.<sup id="cite_ref-Houseman1969_52-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Houseman1969-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> Many ballads were brought by English settlers to the <a href="/wiki/New_World" title="New World">New World</a>, thus forming the bedrock of American folk music.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Carols">Carols</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Folk_music_of_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Carols">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Carols" class="mw-redirect" title="Carols">Carols</a></div>\n<p>A carol is a festive song, in modern times recognised as being exclusively associated with Christmas, but in reality there are carols celebrating all festivals and seasons of the year and not necessarily Christian festivals. They were derived from a form of <a href="/wiki/Circle_dance" title="Circle dance">circle dance</a> accompanied by singers, which was popular from the mid-12th century.<sup id="cite_ref-Walsh2001_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Walsh2001-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> From the 14th century they were used as processional songs, particularly at Advent, Easter and Christmas, and to accompany religious <a href="/wiki/Mystery_plays" class="mw-redirect" title="Mystery plays">mystery plays</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Phillips2008_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Phillips2008-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup>  They declined after the <a href="/wiki/Protestant_Reformation" class="mw-redirect" title="Protestant Reformation">Protestant Reformation</a> which banned many religious festivals, but some famous carols were written in this period, including \'<a href="/wiki/The_Holly_and_the_Ivy" title="The Holly and the Ivy">The Holly and the Ivy</a>\' and they were more strongly revived from the 19th century and began to be written and adapted by eminent composers.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span id="Children.27s_songs"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Children\'s_songs">Children\'s songs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Folk_music_of_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Children&#039;s songs">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Children%27s_songs" class="mw-redirect" title="Children&#39;s songs">Children\'s songs</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:NewberyPocketBook.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/NewberyPocketBook.jpg/220px-NewberyPocketBook.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/NewberyPocketBook.jpg/330px-NewberyPocketBook.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/NewberyPocketBook.jpg/440px-NewberyPocketBook.jpg 2x" data-file-width="600" data-file-height="451" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:NewberyPocketBook.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>John Newbery\'s <i>A Little Pretty Pocket Book</i></div></div></div>\n<p>The earliest vernacular children\'s songs in Europe are <a href="/wiki/Lullaby" title="Lullaby">lullabies</a> from the later medieval period.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> From soon after we have records of short children\'s rhyming songs, but most nursery rhymes were not written down until the 18th century.<sup id="cite_ref-Opie1997_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Opie1997-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> The first English collections were <i><a href="/wiki/Tommy_Thumb%27s_Song_Book" title="Tommy Thumb&#39;s Song Book">Tommy Thumb\'s Song Book</a></i> and a sequel, <i><a href="/wiki/Tommy_Thumb%27s_Pretty_Song_Book" title="Tommy Thumb&#39;s Pretty Song Book">Tommy Thumb\'s Pretty Song Book</a></i>, are both thought to have been published before 1744, and <a href="/wiki/John_Newbery" title="John Newbery">John Newbery</a>\'s, <i>Mother Goose\'s Melody, or, Sonnets for the Cradle</i> (c.1785), is the first record we have of many classic rhymes.<sup id="cite_ref-Carpenter1984_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Carpenter1984-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> These rhymes seem to have come from a variety of sources, including traditional <a href="/wiki/Riddles" class="mw-redirect" title="Riddles">riddles</a>, <a href="/wiki/Proverb" title="Proverb">proverbs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ballads" class="mw-redirect" title="Ballads">ballads</a>, lines of <a href="/wiki/Mummers" class="mw-redirect" title="Mummers">Mummers</a>\' plays, drinking songs, historical events, and, it has been suggested, ancient pagan rituals.<sup id="cite_ref-Carpenter1984_58-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Carpenter1984-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> Roughly half of the current body recognised \'traditional\' English rhymes were known by the mid-18th century.<sup id="cite_ref-Opie1997_57-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Opie1997-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> From this period we sometimes know the origins and authors of rhymes, like \'<a href="/wiki/Twinkle_Twinkle_Little_Star" class="mw-redirect" title="Twinkle Twinkle Little Star">Twinkle Twinkle Little Star</a>\', which combined an 18th-century French tune with a poem by English writer <a href="/wiki/Jane_Taylor_(poet)" title="Jane Taylor (poet)">Jane Taylor</a> and \'<a href="/wiki/Mary_Had_a_Little_Lamb" title="Mary Had a Little Lamb">Mary Had a Little Lamb</a>\', written by Sarah Josepha Hale of Boston in 1830.<sup id="cite_ref-Carpenter1984_58-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Carpenter1984-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> The first, and possibly the most important collection to focus in this area was, <a href="/wiki/James_Orchard_Halliwell" class="mw-redirect" title="James Orchard Halliwell">James Orchard Halliwell</a>\'s, <i>The Nursery Rhymes of England</i> (1842) and <i>Popular Rhymes and Tales</i> in 1849.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> At the height of the revival <a href="/wiki/Sabine_Baring-Gould" title="Sabine Baring-Gould">Sabine Baring-Gould</a> produced <i>A Book of Nursery Songs</i> (1895), and <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Lang" title="Andrew Lang">Andrew Lang</a> produced <i>The Nursery Rhyme Book</i> in 1897.<sup id="cite_ref-Opie1997_57-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Opie1997-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> Children\'s songs, unlike folk songs, have remained part of a living and continuous tradition, for although added to from other sources and affected by written versions, most adults pass on songs they learned from oral sources as children.<sup id="cite_ref-Carpenter1984_58-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Carpenter1984-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Erotic_folk_songs">Erotic folk songs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Folk_music_of_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Erotic folk songs">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>It has been noted by most recent commentators on English folk song, that love, the erotic and even the pornographic, were major traditional themes and, if more than ballads are considered, may have been the largest groups of printed songs.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> Many collectors in the first revival either ignored such songs, or <a href="/wiki/Bowdlerized" class="mw-redirect" title="Bowdlerized">bowdlerized</a> them for publication, as Francis Child and Cecil Sharp did in their collections.<sup id="cite_ref-Shiach1989_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Shiach1989-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> In the second revival, erotic folk song was much more accepted as part of the canon of traditional song, helped by the publication of books such as Gershon Legman\xe2\x80\x99s, <i>The Horn Book: Studies in Erotic Folklore</i> (1964) and Ed Cray\xe2\x80\x99s, <i>The Erotic Muse: American Bawdy Songs</i>, which printed many previously unpublished songs (1968).<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> In England A. L. Lloyd was the key figure in introducing erotic songs to the canon, lecturing and publishing on the subject. He recorded <i>The Foggy Dew and Other Traditional English Love Songs</i> in 1959, and then <i>The Bird in the Bush, Traditional Erotic Songs</i> in 1966 with <a href="/wiki/Frankie_Armstrong" title="Frankie Armstrong">Frankie Armstrong</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Anne_Briggs" title="Anne Briggs">Anne Briggs</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> He drew a distinction between erotic songs, i.e. those that dealt with love and suggested sexuality through innuendo (like \'The Bonny Black Hare\' and \'The Bird in the Bush\'), and pornographic songs that were explicit and therefore unworthy of attention.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> Some authors, however, find these distinctions more difficult to maintain. Although erotic songs became part of the standard fare in folk clubs and among folk rock musicians, relatively few of the more explicit songs have been placed on record.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hornpipes">Hornpipes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Folk_music_of_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Hornpipes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Hornpipe" title="Hornpipe">Hornpipe</a></div>\n<p>The hornpipe is a style of dance music thought to have taken its name from an English reed instrument by at least the 17th century.<sup id="cite_ref-Ling1997_10-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ling1997-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> In the mid-18th century it changed from 3/2 time to 2/2, assuming its modern character, and probably reaching the height of its popularity as it became a staple of theatrical performances.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> It is most often associated with the <a href="/wiki/Sailor%27s_Hornpipe" class="mw-redirect" title="Sailor&#39;s Hornpipe">Sailor\'s Hornpipe</a>, but has formed the basis of many individual and group <a href="/wiki/Country_dance" title="Country dance">country dances</a> into the modern period.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> Like many dances it was taken up in Scotland and Ireland and given a distinctive national character and moved to America with emigration.<sup id="cite_ref-Lee2006_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lee2006-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Jigs">Jigs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Folk_music_of_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Jigs">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Jigs" class="mw-redirect" title="Jigs">Jigs</a></div>\n<p>Jigs are a style of dance music developed in England to accompany a lively dance with steps, turns and leaps. The term jig was derived from the French \'giguer\', meaning \'to jump\'.<sup id="cite_ref-Ling1997_10-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ling1997-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> It was known as a dance in the 16th century, often in 2/4 time and the term was used for a dancing entertainment in 16th century plays.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> The dance began to be associated with music particularly in 6/8 time, and with slip jigs 9/8 time.<sup id="cite_ref-Lee2006_68-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lee2006-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> In the 17th century the dance was adopted in Ireland and Scotland, where they were widely adapted, and with which countries they are now most often associated.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup>  In some, usually more northern, parts of England, these dances would be referred to as a "Gallop" - such as the Winster Gallop from Derbyshire (though this owes its origins to the Winster Morris).\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Morris_dance">Morris dance</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Folk_music_of_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Morris dance">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Morris_dance" title="Morris dance">Morris dance</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Will_Kemp_Elizabethan_Clown_Jig.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Will_Kemp_Elizabethan_Clown_Jig.jpg/220px-Will_Kemp_Elizabethan_Clown_Jig.jpg" width="220" height="162" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Will_Kemp_Elizabethan_Clown_Jig.jpg/330px-Will_Kemp_Elizabethan_Clown_Jig.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Will_Kemp_Elizabethan_Clown_Jig.jpg/440px-Will_Kemp_Elizabethan_Clown_Jig.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2912" data-file-height="2144" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Will_Kemp_Elizabethan_Clown_Jig.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>English Elizabethan clown Will Kempe dancing a jig from Norwich to London in 1600</div></div></div><p>A morris dance is a type of English folk dance, usually accompanied by music, and based on rhythmic stepping and the execution of choreographed figures by a group of dancers, often using implements such as sticks, swords, and handkerchiefs. The name is thought  to derive from the term \'moorish dance\', for Spanish (Muslim) styles of dance and may derive from English court dances of the period.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> References have been found that suggest that morris dance dates back to the mid-15th century, but claims of pre-Christian origins are now largely dismissed.<sup id="cite_ref-Forrest1999_3-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Forrest1999-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> Morris dance appears to have been widespread in England by the early 17th century, particularly in pastoral areas, but was suppressed, along with associated festivals during and after the <a href="/wiki/English_Civil_War" title="English Civil War">English Civil War</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> It recovered after the <a href="/wiki/English_Restoration" class="mw-redirect" title="English Restoration">Restoration</a> in 1660 but was in steep decline after agricultural and industrial revolutions by the 19th century, when collectors like Cecil Sharp recorded the practice, particularly from versions of dance he found in the <a href="/wiki/Cotswolds" title="Cotswolds">Cotswolds</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Boyes1993_11-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Boyes1993-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> This led to a revival of the tradition, although it may also have affected form and practice.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> Morris dance took something of a back seat to unaccompanied singing in the second revival, but received a further boost when it attracted the attention of British folk rock musicians like Ashley Hutchings, who produced several albums of dance music, including the influential <i><a href="/wiki/Morris_On" title="Morris On">Morris On</a></i> series from 1972.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> Traditionally Morris dance was accompanied by either a <a href="/wiki/Pipe_and_tabor" title="Pipe and tabor">pipe and tabor</a> or a <a href="/wiki/Musical_styles_(violin)#Fiddle" class="mw-redirect" title="Musical styles (violin)">fiddle</a>, but from the mid-19th century most common instruments were the <a href="/wiki/Melodeon_(accordion)" class="mw-redirect" title="Melodeon (accordion)">melodeon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Accordion" title="Accordion">accordion</a>, <a href="/wiki/Concertina" title="Concertina">concertina</a> and <a href="/wiki/Drums" class="mw-redirect" title="Drums">drums</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup> Particularly in Cotswold and Border morris, many tunes are linked to particular dances. Morris dance survives in the distinct local traditions of Cotswold morris, north-west morris, <a href="/wiki/Border_Morris" title="Border Morris">Border Morris</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rapper_dance" class="mw-redirect" title="Rapper dance">rapper dance</a> and <a href="/wiki/Long_Sword_dance" title="Long Sword dance">Long Sword dance</a>.\n</p><h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Protest_songs">Protest songs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Folk_music_of_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Protest songs">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Protest_songs#Early_protest_songs_from_Britain" class="mw-redirect" title="Protest songs">Protest songs \xc2\xa7&#160;Early protest songs from Britain</a></div>\n<p>Perhaps the oldest clear example of an English protest song is the rhyme \xe2\x80\x98When Adam delved and Eve span, who was then the gentleman?\xe2\x80\x99, used in the <a href="/wiki/Peasants_Revolt" class="mw-redirect" title="Peasants Revolt">Peasants Revolt</a> of 1381.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> Songs that celebrated social bandits like <a href="/wiki/Robin_Hood" title="Robin Hood">Robin Hood</a>, from the 14th century onwards can be seen as a more subtle form of protest.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> With the <a href="/wiki/Levellers" title="Levellers">Levellers</a> and <a href="/wiki/Diggers" title="Diggers">Diggers</a> in the mid-17th century, more overt criticism surfaced, as in the ballad "The <a href="/wiki/Diggers%27_Song" title="Diggers&#39; Song">Diggers\' Song</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup> From roughly the same period, songs of protest at war, pointing out the costs to human lives, also begin to appear, like "The Maunding Souldier or The Fruits of Warre is Beggery", framed as a begging appeal from a crippled soldier of the <a href="/wiki/Thirty_Years_War" class="mw-redirect" title="Thirty Years War">Thirty Years War</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-SolaPinto1957_79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SolaPinto1957-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> With industrialisation from the 18th century.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> A surprising English folk hero immortalised in song is Napoleon Bonaparte, in songs such as the "Bonny Bunch of Roses" and "Napoleon\xe2\x80\x99s Dream".<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup> As labour became more organised songs were used as anthems and propaganda, for miners with songs like "The Black Leg Miner", and for factory workers with songs like "The Factory Bell".<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> These industrial protest songs were largely ignored during the first English folk revival of the later 19th and early 20th century, but were recorded by figures like <a href="/wiki/A._L._Lloyd" title="A. L. Lloyd">A. L.
