Lloyd</a> on albums such as <i>The Iron Muse</i> (1963).<sup id="cite_ref-Sweers2005pp32-6_19-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sweers2005pp32-6-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> In the 1980s the anarchist rock band <a href="/wiki/Chumbawamba" title="Chumbawamba">Chumbawamba</a> recorded several versions of traditional English protest as <i><a href="/wiki/English_Rebel_Songs_1381%E2%80%931914" class="mw-redirect" title="English Rebel Songs 1381\xe2\x80\x931914">English Rebel Songs 1381\xe2\x80\x931914</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Ewan_MacColl" title="Ewan MacColl">Ewan MacColl</a> became the leading writer of English protest songs in the 1950s, with pro-communist songs such as "The Ballad of <a href="/wiki/Ho_Chi_Minh" title="Ho Chi Minh">Ho Chi Minh</a>" and "The Ballad of <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Stalin" title="Joseph Stalin">Stalin</a>", as well as volatile protest and topical songs concerning the nuclear threat to peace, most notably "Against the Atom Bomb".<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup> The leading voice of protest in <a href="/wiki/Margaret_Thatcher" title="Margaret Thatcher">Thatcherite</a> Britain in the 1980s was <a href="/wiki/Billy_Bragg" title="Billy Bragg">Billy Bragg</a>, whose style of protest song and grass-roots political activism was mostly reminiscent of those of <a href="/wiki/Woody_Guthrie" title="Woody Guthrie">Woody Guthrie</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sea_shanties">Sea shanties</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Folk_music_of_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Sea shanties">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Sea_shanties" class="mw-redirect" title="Sea shanties">Sea shanties</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Capstan.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Capstan.jpg/220px-Capstan.jpg" width="220" height="278" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Capstan.jpg/330px-Capstan.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Capstan.jpg/440px-Capstan.jpg 2x" data-file-width="750" data-file-height="948" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Capstan.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Sailors working at a capstan with musical accompaniment</div></div></div><p>Sea shanties are a type of <a href="/wiki/Work_song" title="Work song">work song</a> traditionally sung by sailors. Derived from the French word \'chanter\', meaning \'to sing\', they may date from as early as the 15th century, but most recorded examples derive from the 19th century.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup> Shanties were usually slow rhythmic songs designed to help with collective tasks on labour-intensive sailing and later steam ships. Many were <a href="/wiki/Call_and_response" title="Call and response">call and response</a> songs, with one voice (the shantyman) singing a lead line and the rest of the sailors giving a response together. There were derived from varied sources, including dances, folk songs, <a href="/wiki/Polka" title="Polka">polkas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Waltz" title="Waltz">waltzes</a> and even West African work-songs.<sup id="cite_ref-Hugill1980_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hugill1980-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> Since different songs were useful for different tasks they are traditionally divided into three main categories, short haul shanties, for tasks requiring quick pulls over a relatively short time; halyard shanties, for heavier work requiring more set-up time between pulls; and <a href="/wiki/Capstan_(nautical)" title="Capstan (nautical)">Capstan</a> shanties, for long, repetitive tasks requiring a sustained rhythm, but not involving working the lines.<sup id="cite_ref-Hugill1980_87-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hugill1980-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> Famous shanties include, the \'<a href="/wiki/Drunken_Sailor" title="Drunken Sailor">Drunken Sailor</a>\' and \'<a href="/wiki/Blow_the_Man_Down" title="Blow the Man Down">Blow the Man Down</a>\'. There was some interest in sea shanties in the first revival from figures like Percy Grainger.<sup id="cite_ref-Bird1999_88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bird1999-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup> In the second revival A. L. Lloyd attempted to popularise them, recording several albums of sea songs from 1965.<sup id="cite_ref-Sweers2005pp32-6_19-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sweers2005pp32-6-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><h3><span class="mw-headline" id="War_songs">War songs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Folk_music_of_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: War songs">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/War_songs" class="mw-redirect" title="War songs">War songs</a></div>\n<p>In England songs about military and naval subjects were a major part of the output of <a href="/wiki/Ballads" class="mw-redirect" title="Ballads">ballad</a> writers from the 16th century onwards, including one of the earliest British ballads \xe2\x80\x98<a href="/wiki/The_Ballad_of_Chevy_Chase" title="The Ballad of Chevy Chase">The Ballad of Chevy Chase</a>\xe2\x80\x99, which deals with the events of the Scottish victory of the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Otterburn" title="Battle of Otterburn">Battle of Otterburn</a> in 1388 and may date to the early 15th century.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup> The conflicts between England and Spain in the later 16th and early 17th centuries produced a number of ballads describing events, particularly naval conflicts like those of the <a href="/wiki/Spanish_Armada" title="Spanish Armada">Spanish Armada</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-SolaPinto1957_79-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SolaPinto1957-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/English_Civil_War" title="English Civil War">English Civil War</a> (1642\xe2\x80\x931653) produced a subgenre of "<a href="/wiki/Cavalier" title="Cavalier">Cavalier</a> ballads", including "When the King Home in Peace Again".<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup> Many of these were adapted and reused by <a href="/wiki/Jacobitism" title="Jacobitism">Jacobites</a> after the \xe2\x80\x98<a href="/wiki/Glorious_Revolution" title="Glorious Revolution">Glorious Revolution</a>\xe2\x80\x99 of 1688.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup> The Anglo-French Wars of the 17th and 18th centuries saw more descriptive works, usually couched in patriotic terms, but some, like \xe2\x80\x98Captain Death\xe2\x80\x99 (1757) dealt with loss and defeat.<sup id="cite_ref-SolaPinto1957_79-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SolaPinto1957-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> As regimental identities emerged songs were adopted for marching, like \xe2\x80\x98<a href="/wiki/The_British_Grenadiers" title="The British Grenadiers">The British Grenadiers</a>\xe2\x80\x99, based on a 17th-century dance tune.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup> Output became a flood during the <a href="/wiki/French_Revolutionary_Wars" title="French Revolutionary Wars">French Revolutionary</a> and <a href="/wiki/Napoleonic_Wars" title="Napoleonic Wars">Napoleonic Wars</a> (1797\xe2\x80\x931815), seeing numerous patriotic war songs, like \xe2\x80\x98<a href="/wiki/Heart_of_Oak" title="Heart of Oak">Heart of Oak</a>\xe2\x80\x99 and the emergence of a stereotype of the English seaman as \xe2\x80\x98Jolly <a href="/wiki/Jack_Tar" title="Jack Tar">Jack Tar</a>\xe2\x80\x99, who appeared in many ballads and on stage.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> As the musical hall began to take over the lead in popular music and folk song declined, folk song ceased to deal with contemporary wars in the later 19th century.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Work_songs">Work songs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Folk_music_of_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Work songs">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Work_songs" class="mw-redirect" title="Work songs">Work songs</a></div>\n<p>Work songs include music sung while conducting a task (often to coordinate timing) or a song linked to a task or trade which might be a connected <a href="/wiki/Narrative" title="Narrative">narrative</a>, description, or <a href="/wiki/Protest_song" title="Protest song">protest song</a>. The two main types of work song in England are agricultural work songs, usually are <a href="/wiki/Rhythmic" class="mw-redirect" title="Rhythmic">rhythmic</a> <a href="/wiki/A_cappella" title="A cappella">a cappella</a> <a href="/wiki/Song" title="Song">songs</a> sung by people working on a physical and often repetitive task, like the \'Harvest song\' common in south-west England.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> The songs were probably intended to increase productivity while reducing feelings of boredom.<sup id="cite_ref-Peek2004_95-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Peek2004-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup> Rhythms of work songs can serve to synchronize physical movement in a group or gang. Industrial folk song emerged in Britain in the 18th century, as workers took the music with which they were familiar, including <a href="/wiki/Ballads" class="mw-redirect" title="Ballads">ballads</a> and agricultural work songs, and adapted them to their new experiences and circumstances.<sup id="cite_ref-Lloyd1967_96-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lloyd1967-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup> Unlike agricultural work songs, it was often unnecessary to use music to synchronise actions between workers, as the pace would be increasingly determined by water, steam, chemical and eventually electric power, and frequently impossible because of the noise of early industry.<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup> As a result, industrial folk songs tended to be descriptive of work, circumstances, or political in nature, making them amongst the earliest <a href="/wiki/Protest_songs" class="mw-redirect" title="Protest songs">protest songs</a> and were sung between work shifts or in leisure hours, rather than during work. This pattern can be seen in <a href="/wiki/Textile_manufacture_during_the_Industrial_Revolution" title="Textile manufacture during the Industrial Revolution">textile production</a>, mining and eventually steel, shipbuilding, rail working and other industries.<sup id="cite_ref-Lloyd1967_96-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lloyd1967-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Regional_traditions">Regional traditions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Folk_music_of_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Regional traditions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="East_Anglia">East Anglia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Folk_music_of_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: East Anglia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:MollyDancers.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/MollyDancers.jpg/220px-MollyDancers.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/MollyDancers.jpg/330px-MollyDancers.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/MollyDancers.jpg/440px-MollyDancers.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="600" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:MollyDancers.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Molly dancers at <a href="/wiki/Whittlesey#Whittlesea_Straw_Bear" title="Whittlesey">Whittlesea Straw Bear</a> Festival</div></div></div>\n<p>Like many regions of England there are few distinctive local instruments and many songs were shared with the rest of Britain and with Ireland, although the distinct dialects of the regions sometimes lent them a particular stamp and, with one of the longest coastlines of any English region, songs about the sea were also particularly important. Along with the West Country, this was one of the regions that most firmly adopted reed instruments, producing many eminent practitioners of the melodeon from the mid-19th century.  Also like the West Country it is one of the few regions where there is still an active tradition of <a href="/wiki/Step_dancing" class="mw-redirect" title="Step dancing">step dancing</a> and like the Midlands the tradition of <a href="/wiki/Molly_dance" title="Molly dance">Molly dance</a> died out in the 1930s.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup> The region was relatively neglected by folk song collectors of the first revival. Lucy Broadwood and Cecil Sharp collected in Cambridgeshire, as did and Vaughan Williams as well as in Norfolk and Essex from 1905, but most important regional figure was composer <a href="/wiki/Ernest_John_Moeran" title="Ernest John Moeran">Ernest John Moeran</a>, who collected over 150 songs in Norfolk and Suffolk in the 1920s.<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup> The second folk revival led to the discovery of many East Anglian folk musicians, including Suffolk melodeon player Oscar Woods, Norfolk singers Sam Larner (1878\xe2\x80\x931965), <a href="/wiki/Harry_Cox" title="Harry Cox">Harry Cox</a> (1885\xe2\x80\x931971) and Walter Pardon (1914\xe2\x80\x9396); Suffolk fiddler Harkie Nesling (1890\xe2\x80\x931978); Suffolk singer and bargeman <a href="/wiki/Bob_Roberts_(folksinger)" title="Bob Roberts (folksinger)">Bob Roberts</a> (1907\xe2\x80\x9382), many of whom recorded for <a href="/wiki/Topic_Records" title="Topic Records">Topic Records</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup> Perhaps the most influential folk dance musical album was <i>English Country Dance Music</i> (1965), put together by Reg Hall and Bob Davenport with largely Norfolk musicians, it was the first instrumental recording of folk instruments.<sup id="cite_ref-Broughton1999_20-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Broughton1999-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> Also from Norfolk was <a href="/wiki/Peter_Bellamy" title="Peter Bellamy">Peter Bellamy</a>, who in solo projects, with the <a href="/wiki/Young_Tradition" class="mw-redirect" title="Young Tradition">Young Tradition</a> and in theatrical productions was probably one of the most influential musicians of the post revival period.<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup> The Norfolk melodeon player and singer Tony Hall has given the tradition a unique style.<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup> East Anglia made a contribution to the British folk rock scene of the 1970s, producing the short-lived, but more recently reformed, bands Midwinter and Stone Angel, based in <a href="/wiki/Great_Yarmouth" title="Great Yarmouth">Great Yarmouth</a> and the more successful <a href="/wiki/Spriguns_of_Tolgus" title="Spriguns of Tolgus">Spriguns of Tolgus</a> from Cambridge, who produced four albums.<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup> The most successful folk artists from the region in recent years are probably the Essex born <a href="/wiki/Billy_Bragg" title="Billy Bragg">Billy Bragg</a> and the Norfolk born <a href="/wiki/Beth_Orton" title="Beth Orton">Beth Orton</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup> The region is home to numerous folk clubs and hosts many folk festivals, including Steeleye Span\xe2\x80\x99s Spanfest at <a href="/wiki/Kentwell_Hall" title="Kentwell Hall">Kentwell Hall</a>, Suffolk and the <a href="/wiki/Cambridge_Folk_Festival" title="Cambridge Folk Festival">Cambridge Folk Festival</a>, generally seen as the most prestigious in the calendar.<sup id="cite_ref-folkandroots.co.uk_51-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-folkandroots.co.uk-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> Since 2000 the East Anglian Traditional Music Trust has been promoting folk music in the region, organising a \xe2\x80\x98Traditional Music Day\xe2\x80\x99 every year in August.<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Midlands">The Midlands</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Folk_music_of_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: The Midlands">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Due to its lack of clear boundaries and a perceived lack of identity in its folk music, the <a href="/wiki/English_Midlands" class="mw-redirect" title="English Midlands">English Midlands</a> attracted relatively little interest in the early revivals. However, in more recent years a distinct cultural heritage has been recognised including unique folk traditions and songs, many associated with the regions industrial connections. It has also produced a number of important performers and some particular local instruments, such as the Lincolnshire <a href="/wiki/Bagpipes" title="Bagpipes">bagpipes</a>, however the last player, John Hunsley, died in the 19th century and no actual examples of the pipes have survived.<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup> From the 19th century the instruments used appear to have been much like those in other regions, with fiddles, accordions and eventually silver and brass. Although, some traditions, like <a href="/wiki/Molly_dance" title="Molly dance">Molly dance</a> died out in the 1930s, the Midlands retained strong traditions of both ceremonial and social dance, particularly in the south Midlands and <a href="/wiki/Cotswolds" title="Cotswolds">Cotswolds</a> and in the distinctive <a href="/wiki/Border_Morris" title="Border Morris">Border Morris</a> from Herefordshire, Worcestershire and Shropshire.<sup id="cite_ref-Broughton1999_20-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Broughton1999-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> The region also furnished some important material for folk songs, including a claim by Nottinghamshire for one of the most popular series of ballads, that of <a href="/wiki/Robin_Hood" title="Robin Hood">Robin Hood</a>, while local places appear in songs such as \xe2\x80\x98The Leicester Chambermaid\xe2\x80\x99 and \xe2\x80\x98Oxford\xe2\x80\x99 or \xe2\x80\x98Worcester City\xe2\x80\x99.<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup> Folk song collecting in the first revival was much less comprehensive than for many other regions. In the 1860s Llewellynn Jewitt, collected songs from Derbyshire, and some songs were printed by Georgina F. Jackson in her study of Shropshire folk lore.<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup> Cecil Sharp\xe2\x80\x99s interest in the region was largely confined to the south, particularly the <a href="/wiki/Cotswold" class="mw-redirect" title="Cotswold">Cotswold</a> morris villages of Oxfordshire and Warwickshire, which provided him with an archetype of English ceremonial dance.<sup id="cite_ref-Boyes1993_11-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Boyes1993-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> From 1905 Percy Grainger was actively collecting in Lincolnshire, acquiring recordings of songs that would provide the basis for his <i><a href="/wiki/Lincolnshire_Posy" title="Lincolnshire Posy">Lincolnshire Posy</a></i> (1937).<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup> It was not until the early 1970s that the broader heritage of the region, including the many industrial and work songs associated with mining or <a href="/wiki/The_Potteries" class="mw-redirect" title="The Potteries">The Potteries</a>, began to gain serious attention.<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup> Despite this neglect there was an active folk scene in the region, which produced several key artists of the second revival from the 1960s, including <a href="/wiki/Anne_Briggs" title="Anne Briggs">Anne Briggs</a> from Nottinghamshire, <a href="/wiki/The_Settlers_(band)" title="The Settlers (band)">The Settlers</a> from the <a href="/wiki/West_Midlands_(region)" title="West Midlands (region)">West Midlands</a> and from Birmingham one of the most influential groups of the period, the Ian Campbell Folk Group, which numbered among its members later British folk rock musicians <a href="/wiki/Dave_Swarbrick" title="Dave Swarbrick">Dave Swarbrick</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dave_Pegg" title="Dave Pegg">Dave Pegg</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Brocken2003_16-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brocken2003-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> Slightly later a number of folk groups came out of Derbyshire, including The Druids, Ram\'s Bottom Band and <a href="/wiki/Muckram_Wakes" title="Muckram Wakes">Muckram Wakes</a>, which included one of the most highly regarded modern performers <a href="/wiki/John_Tams" title="John Tams">John Tams</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Brocken2003_16-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brocken2003-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> Lincolnshire has produced <a href="/wiki/Martin_Simpson" title="Martin Simpson">Martin Simpson</a>, perhaps the most highly regarded folk guitarist of his generation.<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup> Birmingham\xe2\x80\x99s position as a centre for folk music has been emphasised by its place as the home of the <a href="/wiki/Birmingham_Conservatoire" class="mw-redirect" title="Birmingham Conservatoire">Birmingham Conservatoire</a> Folk Ensemble, led by former <a href="/wiki/Albion_Band" class="mw-redirect" title="Albion Band">Albion Band</a> fiddler Joe Broughton, which provides something of a clearing house of promising young folk musicians.<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup> The regions has numerous folk clubs and host many major folk festivals, including those of <a href="/wiki/Gainsborough,_Lincolnshire" title="Gainsborough, Lincolnshire">Gainsborough, Lincolnshire</a>, <a href="/wiki/Loughborough" title="Loughborough">Loughborough</a> in Leicestershire, <a href="/wiki/Shrewsbury" title="Shrewsbury">Shrewsbury</a> in Shropshire, <a href="/wiki/Warwick" title="Warwick">Warwick</a> and Moseley.<sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_North_West">The North West</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Folk_music_of_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: The North West">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Although relatively neglected in the first folk revival <a href="/wiki/North_West_England" title="North West England">North West England</a> had a rich tradition of balladry stretching back at least to the 17th century and sharing in the tradition of <a href="/wiki/Border_ballads" class="mw-redirect" title="Border ballads">Border ballads</a>, including perhaps the finest \xe2\x80\x98<a href="/wiki/The_Ballad_of_Chevy_Chase" title="The Ballad of Chevy Chase">The Ballad of Chevy Chase</a>\', thought to have been composed by the Lancashire-born sixteenth century minstrel Richard Sheale.<sup id="cite_ref-Gregory2006_114-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gregory2006-114">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup> Lancashire in particular was a common location for folk songs, including \xe2\x80\x98The Lancashire Miller\xe2\x80\x99, \xe2\x80\x98Warrington Ale\xe2\x80\x99 and \xe2\x80\x98The soldier\xe2\x80\x99s farewell to Manchester\xe2\x80\x99, beside several local <a href="/wiki/Wassailing" title="Wassailing">Wassailing</a> songs.<sup id="cite_ref-Gregory2006_114-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gregory2006-114">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup> With a variety of dialects and acting as something of a crossroads for the cultures and immigrants of England, Scotland and Ireland, there is a distinctive local character to folk music, which expressed itself in local enthusiasm that emerged as a major factor within the wider folk movement in the second revival. The key event in the history of folk music in the counties of the north west of England was the Industrial Revolution, which divided the region economically and culturally into a northern, often highland and pastoral region, in Westmorland and Cumberland and a more urbanised and industrialised southern zone with large and growing conurbations like Manchester and Liverpool, where changing social and economic patterns emerged in new traditions and styles of folk song, often linked to migration and patterns of work, these included processional dances, often associated with rushbearing and the <a href="/wiki/Wakes_Week" class="mw-redirect" title="Wakes Week">Wakes Week</a> festivities and types of <a href="/wiki/Step_dance" title="Step dance">step dance</a>, most famously <a href="/wiki/Clog_dancing" title="Clog dancing">clog dancing</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup> These were very different from the styles of dance that collectors like Cecil Sharp had encountered in the Cotswolds and were largely dismissed by him as contaminated by urbanisation, yet they were, and remain, a thriving tradition of music and dance.<sup id="cite_ref-Boyes1993_11-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Boyes1993-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> A local pioneer of folk song collection in the first half of the 19th century in Lancashire was Shakespearian scholar <a href="/wiki/James_Orchard_Halliwell" class="mw-redirect" title="James Orchard Halliwell">James Orchard Halliwell</a>, and he was followed a little later by <a href="/wiki/John_Harland" title="John Harland">John Harland</a>, William E. Axon, Thomas T. Wilkinson and Sidney Gilpin, who performed a similar service for Cumberland.<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup> Most of these works, although important in unearthing, and in some cases preserving, locally relevant ballads, largely depended on manuscript sources, rather than oral collection and often did not give tunes, but only lyrics.<sup id="cite_ref-Gregory2006_114-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gregory2006-114">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup> It was not until the second folk revival that the full range of song from the region began to gain attention. The region not only produced one of the major figures of the revival in Ewan MacColl but also a local champion in <a href="/wiki/Harry_Boardman" title="Harry Boardman">Harry Boardman</a>, who from 1965 onwards probably did more than anyone to popularise and record the industrial folk song of the region, in several albums and books.<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup> The region produced no significant bands in the folk rock movement of the 1970s but can claim one of the most significant figures, as Maddy Prior was brought up in Blackpool. However, perhaps the most influential folk artists to emerge from the region in this period were folk troubadour <a href="/wiki/Roy_Harper_(singer)" title="Roy Harper (singer)">Roy Harper</a> and comedian and broadcaster <a href="/wiki/Mike_Harding" title="Mike Harding">Mike Harding</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup> More recently it has produced some significant performers including guitarist <a href="/wiki/Ken_Nicol_(musician)" title="Ken Nicol (musician)">Ken Nicol</a> and mother and daughter singer songwriters <a href="/wiki/Chris_While" title="Chris While">Chris</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kellie_While" title="Kellie While">Kellie While</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup> The region is home to numerous folk clubs, many of them catering to Irish and Scots folk. Folk festivals include the Fylde Folk Festival at <a href="/wiki/Fleetwood" title="Fleetwood">Fleetwood</a> in Lancashire.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_120-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-120">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Northumbria">Northumbria</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Folk_music_of_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Northumbria">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Music_of_Northumbria" title="Music of Northumbria">Music of Northumbria</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Billy_Purvis_(1784-1853).JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/a/a4/Billy_Purvis_%281784-1853%29.JPG/220px-Billy_Purvis_%281784-1853%29.JPG" width="220" height="293" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/a/a4/Billy_Purvis_%281784-1853%29.JPG/330px-Billy_Purvis_%281784-1853%29.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/a/a4/Billy_Purvis_%281784-1853%29.JPG/440px-Billy_Purvis_%281784-1853%29.JPG 2x" data-file-width="960" data-file-height="1280" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Billy_Purvis_(1784-1853).JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Billy Purvis (1784\xe2\x80\x931853) one of the last travelling minstrel pipers of the south of Scotland and the north east of England.</div></div></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Northumbria" class="mw-redirect" title="Northumbria">Northumbria</a> possesses a distinctive style of folk music with a flourishing and continuing tradition.<sup id="cite_ref-Broughton1999_20-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Broughton1999-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> The region is particularly noted for the unique <a href="/wiki/Northumbrian_smallpipes" title="Northumbrian smallpipes">Northumbrian smallpipes</a> and strong <a href="/wiki/Fiddle" title="Fiddle">fiddle</a> tradition that was already well-established in the 1690s. Northumbrian music is characterised by considerable influence from other regions, particularly southern <a href="/wiki/Scotland" title="Scotland">Scotland</a>, other parts of the north of England and <a href="/wiki/Music_of_Ireland" title="Music of Ireland">Ireland</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Broughton1999_20-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Broughton1999-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> Local tunes were collected from the mid-18th century by figures including Henry Atkinson and William Vickers and in the first revival by John Bell, Bruce. J. Collingwood and John Stokoe.<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup> The short-lived <a href="/wiki/Northumbrian_Small_Pipes_Society" title="Northumbrian Small Pipes Society">Northumbrian Small Pipes Society</a> was founded in Newcastle in 1893 and the <a href="/wiki/Northumbrian_Pipers%27_Society" title="Northumbrian Pipers&#39; Society">Northumbrian Pipers\' Society</a> in 1928, and they are generally credited with keeping the distinctive tradition alive.<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup> Border ballads were a major part of those collected by <a href="/wiki/Francis_James_Child" title="Francis James Child">Francis James Child</a> and make up most of the sixth volume of his ten volume collection of <i><a href="/wiki/Child_ballads" class="mw-redirect" title="Child ballads">The English and Scottish Popular Ballads</a></i> (1882\xe2\x80\x9398).<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup> The second folk revival saw a number of acts drawing on this work, and enjoying some success. Probably the most influential piper at that time was <a href="/wiki/Billy_Pigg" title="Billy Pigg">Billy Pigg</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Connell2003_18-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Connell2003-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> Performers such Louis Killen, The <a href="/wiki/High_Level_Ranters" class="mw-redirect" title="High Level Ranters">High Level Ranters</a> and Bob Davenport brought Northumbrian folk to national and international audiences.<sup id="cite_ref-Broughton1999_20-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Broughton1999-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> The 1970s saw folk rock bands like <a href="/wiki/Lindisfarne_(band)" title="Lindisfarne (band)">Lindisfarne</a>, and the more traditionally focused <a href="/wiki/Jack_the_Lad" title="Jack the Lad">Jack the Lad</a> and  <a href="/wiki/Hedgehog_Pie" title="Hedgehog Pie">Hedgehog Pie</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Broughton1999_20-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Broughton1999-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> More recently, Northumbrian folk music, and particularly the use of the Northumbrian pipes, has become one of the liveliest and most widely known subgenres of folk music in Britain, with artists like fiddler <a href="/wiki/Nancy_Kerr" title="Nancy Kerr">Nancy Kerr</a>, piper <a href="/wiki/Kathryn_Tickell" title="Kathryn Tickell">Kathryn Tickell</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rachel_Unthank_and_the_Winterset" class="mw-redirect" title="Rachel Unthank and the Winterset">Rachel Unthank and the Winterset</a> gaining international reputations.<sup id="cite_ref-Broughton1999_20-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Broughton1999-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> Currently the region has over thirty active folk clubs and hosts several major folk festivals, including the Traditional Music Festival at <a href="/wiki/Rothbury" title="Rothbury">Rothbury</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_South_East">The South East</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Folk_music_of_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: The South East">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Even excluding Sussex and London, <a href="/wiki/South-east_England" class="mw-redirect" title="South-east England">South-east England</a> has been one of the key areas of English folk music and collection. It had retained a strong tradition of wassailing, and seafaring songs were important in the coastal counties of Kent and Hampshire. Arguably the published collection of oral material was made in this area by John Broadwood, as <i>Old English Songs, As Now Sung by the Peasantry of the Weald of Surrey and Sussex</i> (1843).<sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup> When the first revival was at its height in the first decade of the 20th century, George Gardiner and Alice Gillington both collected songs in Hampshire, Lucy Broadwood in Surrey, Hampshire and Oxfordshire, <a href="/wiki/Alfred_Williams_(poet)" title="Alfred Williams (poet)">Alfred Williams</a> in Oxfordshire and Berkshire and Cecil Sharp in Berkshire, Buckinghamshire, and Kent.<sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup> In the second folk revival the region contributed several figures, with probably the most important being Martin Carthy from Hertfordshire. The most significant British folk rock group from the region were the <a href="/wiki/Oyster_Band" class="mw-redirect" title="Oyster Band">Oyster Band</a>, formed in Canterbury, while guitarist John Martyn came from Surrey and fiddle player Chris Leslie from Banbury in Oxfordshire. From the current crop of young folk musicians probably the most prominent are <a href="/wiki/Spiers_and_Boden" title="Spiers and Boden">Spiers and Boden</a> from Oxfordshire and <a href="/wiki/Chris_Wood_(folk_musician)" title="Chris Wood (folk musician)">Chris Wood</a>, born in Kent. The region is host to numerous folk clubs, and festivals, including the Oxford festival and Fairport\xe2\x80\x99s Cropredy Convention in Oxfordshire and St Albans in Hertfordshire.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_120-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-120">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="London">London</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Folk_music_of_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: London">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Streetcries.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Streetcries.jpg/220px-Streetcries.jpg" width="220" height="153" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Streetcries.jpg/330px-Streetcries.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Streetcries.jpg/440px-Streetcries.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="557" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Streetcries.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Street vendors in a 16th-century print</div></div></div>\n<p>Despite being the centre of both folk revivals and the British folk rock movement, the songs of <a href="/wiki/London" title="London">London</a> were largely neglected in favour of regional and rural music until relatively recently. London, unsurprisingly, was the most common location mentioned in English folk songs, including \xe2\x80\x98London is a Fine Town\xe2\x80\x99, and the \xe2\x80\x98London Prentice\xe2\x80\x99 and it was the centre of the broadside publishing industry.<sup id="cite_ref-SolaPinto1957_79-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SolaPinto1957-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> From the 17th century to the 19th, street singers were characteristic of London life, often selling printed versions of the songs they sang.<sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup> The capital was home to the Folk-Song Society and the English Folk Dance Society from the late 19th century, but the most distinctive genre of London music, its many <a href="/wiki/Street_cries" title="Street cries">street cries</a>, were not considered folk music by mainstream collectors and were recorded and published by figures such as Andrew White in <i>Old London Street Cries&#160;; and, The Cries of To-day</i> (1885).<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup> Both Ewan MacColl and A. L. Lloyd gravitated to London in the 1950s, it was the base of Topic Records and it was there that the first folk clubs were formed before they spread out across the country.<sup id="cite_ref-Sweers2005pp45-9_4-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sweers2005pp45-9-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> It was also the home of folk musicians like Ashley Hutchings, Richard Thompson and Simon Nicol who formed Fairport Convention, and many artists, like Bert Jansch and Davy Graham, moved there in order to be able to pursue their careers or for the greater networks and opportunities the capital allowed.<sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup> More recent performers of folk music include <a href="/wiki/Noah_and_the_Whale" title="Noah and the Whale">Noah and the Whale</a>, Emma Lee Moss, <a href="/wiki/Mumford_and_Sons" class="mw-redirect" title="Mumford and Sons">Mumford and Sons</a> and <a href="/wiki/The_Border_Surrender" title="The Border Surrender">The Border Surrender</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Sussex">Sussex</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Folk_music_of_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Sussex">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Music_of_Sussex" title="Music of Sussex">Music of Sussex</a></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Sussex" title="Sussex">Sussex</a> has disproportionately affected the history of English folk music. This was due to a flourishing tradition of <a href="/wiki/Folk_dance" title="Folk dance">folk dance</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mummers" class="mw-redirect" title="Mummers">mummers</a> plays and folk song, but also in part because of the rural nature of the county in the late 19th and early 20th centuries and yet its relatively close proximity to London. It was thus a rich and convenient place for the collectors of the first folk song revival, including <a href="/wiki/Kate_Lee_(English_singer)" title="Kate Lee (English singer)">Kate Lee</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lucy_Broadwood" title="Lucy Broadwood">Lucy Broadwood</a> and W. P. Merrick.<sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup> Sussex material was used by the composers of the English pastoral school, for example in <a href="/wiki/Percy_Grainger" title="Percy Grainger">Percy Grainger</a>\xe2\x80\x99s arrangement of \xe2\x80\x98The Sussex Mummers\' Christmas Carol\xe2\x80\x99, <a href="/wiki/Ralph_Vaughan_Williams" title="Ralph Vaughan Williams">Ralph Vaughan Williams</a>\' use of the tune \xe2\x80\x98<a href="/wiki/Monk%27s_Gate" title="Monk&#39;s Gate">Monk\'s Gate</a>\xe2\x80\x99 as a setting for <a href="/wiki/John_Bunyan" title="John Bunyan">John Bunyan</a>\xe2\x80\x99s \xe2\x80\x98<a href="/wiki/To_be_a_Pilgrim" class="mw-redirect" title="To be a Pilgrim">To be a Pilgrim</a>\xe2\x80\x99 and <a href="/wiki/George_Butterworth" title="George Butterworth">George Butterworth</a>\xe2\x80\x99s arrangement of \'Folk Songs from Sussex\'.<sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup> Most important of the collector\xe2\x80\x99s sources were the <a href="/wiki/Copper_Family" title="Copper Family">Copper Family</a> of <a href="/wiki/Rottingdean" title="Rottingdean">Rottingdean</a>, who emerged as authorities on folk song and eventually as major recording artists.<sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup> Sussex folk song also had a formative effect on one of the major figures of the second revival, as it was as a child of five in Sussex that A. L. Lloyd first heard folk music.<sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup> Other performers include <a href="/wiki/Scan_Tester" title="Scan Tester">Scan Tester</a>, <a href="/wiki/Henry_Burstow" title="Henry Burstow">Henry Burstow</a> and the sisters <a href="/wiki/Dolly_Collins" title="Dolly Collins">Dolly</a> and <a href="/wiki/Shirley_Collins" title="Shirley Collins">Shirley Collins</a>. Sussex songs were also the foundation of the repertoire of the influential <a href="/wiki/Young_Tradition" class="mw-redirect" title="Young Tradition">Young Tradition</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup> The county has over twenty folk clubs and other venues hosting folk music by organisations such as <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.acousticsussex.org.uk/">Acoustic Sussex</a>. There are also annual folk music festivals at <a href="/wiki/Eastbourne" title="Eastbourne">Eastbourne</a>, <a href="/wiki/Crawley" title="Crawley">Crawley</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lewes" title="Lewes">Lewes</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_West_Country">The West Country</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Folk_music_of_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: The West Country">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Cornwall">Cornwall</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Folk_music_of_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Cornwall">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Music_of_Cornwall" title="Music of Cornwall">Music of Cornwall</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Red_obby_oss_party_20050502.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Red_obby_oss_party_20050502.jpg/220px-Red_obby_oss_party_20050502.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Red_obby_oss_party_20050502.jpg/330px-Red_obby_oss_party_20050502.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Red_obby_oss_party_20050502.jpg/440px-Red_obby_oss_party_20050502.jpg 2x" data-file-width="600" data-file-height="400" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Red_obby_oss_party_20050502.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The red party attending the red \'obby \'oss in the Padstow mayday festival.</div></div></div>\n<p>The music of <a href="/wiki/Cornwall" title="Cornwall">Cornwall</a> is often noted for its similarity to that of <a href="/wiki/Music_of_Brittany" title="Music of Brittany">Brittany</a> and, as a result of the close physical and cultural ties between the two peninsulas, some older songs and carols share the same root as <a href="/wiki/Breton_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Breton people">Breton</a> tunes.<sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup> From the late Middle Ages the <a href="/wiki/Fiddle" title="Fiddle">fiddle</a> (<i>crowd</i> in Cornish), <a href="/wiki/Bombarde" class="mw-redirect" title="Bombarde">bombarde</a> (<i>horn-pipe</i>), <a href="/wiki/Bagpipes" title="Bagpipes">bagpipes</a> and <a href="/wiki/Harp" title="Harp">harp</a> all seem to have been used in music. The <a href="/wiki/Cornish_bagpipes" title="Cornish bagpipes">Cornish bagpipes</a> died out, as elsewhere in southern England, in the 16th century, but have recently been re-created.<sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup> From the mid-19th century <a href="/wiki/Accordion" title="Accordion">accordions</a> became progressively more popular as a folk instrument in the county, as in the rest of the West Country. There is long and varied history of <a href="/wiki/Cornish_dance" title="Cornish dance">Cornish dance</a> from the medieval period, with records of strong traditions of  <a href="/wiki/Morris_dancing" class="mw-redirect" title="Morris dancing">morris dancing</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mumming" class="mw-redirect" title="Mumming">mumming</a>, <a href="/wiki/Guise_dancing" title="Guise dancing">guise dancing</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Social_dance" title="Social dance">social dance</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup> These seem to have been interrupted by the <a href="/wiki/English_Reformation" title="English Reformation">Reformation</a> and <a href="/wiki/English_Civil_War" title="English Civil War">Civil War</a> and Commonwealth in the 16th and 17th centuries.<sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup> However, there was  revival from the late 18th century and  seasonal and community festivals, mumming and <a href="/wiki/Guise_dancing" title="Guise dancing">guising</a> all flourished.<sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup> In the 19th century a strong tradition of nonconformity and temperance may also have affected dancing and music adversely and encouraged choral and brass band movements, while traditional tunes were used for carols. Some community events survived, such as the <a href="/wiki/%27Obby_%27Oss_festival" title="&#39;Obby &#39;Oss festival">\'Obby \'Oss festival</a> in <a href="/wiki/Padstow" title="Padstow">Padstow</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Furry_Dance" title="Furry Dance">Furry Dance</a> in <a href="/wiki/Helston" title="Helston">Helston</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup> Folk songs include \xe2\x80\x98<a href="/wiki/Sweet_Nightingale" title="Sweet Nightingale">Sweet Nightingale</a>\xe2\x80\x99, \xe2\x80\x98<a href="/wiki/Little_Eyes" title="Little Eyes">Little Eyes</a>\xe2\x80\x99, and \xe2\x80\x98<a href="/wiki/Lamorna_(folk_song)" title="Lamorna (folk song)">Lamorna</a>\xe2\x80\x99. \'<a href="/wiki/The_Song_of_the_Western_Men" title="The Song of the Western Men">Trelawny</a>\' is often sung at sporting events and is seen by many as an unofficial anthem.<sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup> Few traditional Cornish lyrics survived the decline of the language, but in some cases lyrics of common English songs became attached to older Cornish tunes.<sup id="cite_ref-Ellis1974_145-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ellis1974-145">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup> Some folk tunes have Cornish lyrics written since the language revival of the 1920s.<sup id="cite_ref-Ellis1974_145-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ellis1974-145">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup> Modern Cornish musicians include the former Cornish folk singer <a href="/wiki/Brenda_Wootton" title="Brenda Wootton">Brenda Wootton</a> and the Cornish-Breton family band Anao Atao.<sup id="cite_ref-Ellis1974_145-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ellis1974-145">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup> Recently bands like Sacred Turf, Skwardya and Krena, have begun performing <a href="/wiki/British_folk_rock" title="British folk rock">British folk rock</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Cornish_language" title="Cornish language">Cornish language</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup> The Cornwall Folk Festival has been held annually for more than three decades.<sup id="cite_ref-147" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-147">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="The_rest_of_the_West_Country">The rest of the West Country</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Folk_music_of_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: The rest of the West Country">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Seth_Lakeman.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Seth_Lakeman.jpg/220px-Seth_Lakeman.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Seth_Lakeman.jpg/330px-Seth_Lakeman.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Seth_Lakeman.jpg/440px-Seth_Lakeman.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="768" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Seth_Lakeman.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Seth Lakeman on stage in 2008</div></div></div>\n<p>Outside Devon and Cornwall Celtic influence on music in the <a href="/wiki/West_Country" title="West Country">West Country</a> is much less obvious, but folk music still retains many distinctive local characteristics. As in Cornwall there are very strong traditions of folk dance and mumming, the best known being the <a href="/wiki/Hobby_horse" title="Hobby horse">Hobby horse</a> celebrations at <a href="/wiki/Minehead" title="Minehead">Minehead</a> in Somerset.<sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148">&#91;148&#93;</a></sup> The maritime heritage of Devon made sea shanties, hornpipes and naval or sea ballads important parts of regional folk music.<sup id="cite_ref-Bird1999_88-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bird1999-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup> From the 19th century <a href="/wiki/Accordions" class="mw-redirect" title="Accordions">accordions</a> have been a popular and accepted part of the local folk sound. Folk songs from the West Country include \xe2\x80\x98<a href="/wiki/Widdecombe_Fair" class="mw-redirect" title="Widdecombe Fair">Widdecombe Fair</a>\xe2\x80\x99, \xe2\x80\x98<a href="/wiki/Spanish_Ladies" title="Spanish Ladies">Spanish Ladies</a>\xe2\x80\x99 and \xe2\x80\x98The Seeds of Love.\xe2\x80\x99 The region was important in the first folk revival, as the Devon-born <a href="/wiki/Antiquarian" title="Antiquarian">antiquarian</a> <a href="/wiki/Sabine_Baring-Gould" title="Sabine Baring-Gould">Sabine Baring-Gould</a> invested effort in collecting regional music, published as <i>Songs and Ballads of the West</i> (1889\xe2\x80\x9391), the first collection published for the mass market. He later collaborated with Cecil Sharp who, with Charles Marson, produced a three volume <i>Folk-Songs from Somerset</i> (1904\xe2\x80\x9309).<sup id="cite_ref-149" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-149">&#91;149&#93;</a></sup> Other collectors included Henry and Robert Hammond in Dorset, the Reverend Geoffrey Hill in Wiltshire, Percy Grainger in Gloucestershire and, perhaps the most famous, Ralph Vaughan Williams\' \'<a href="/wiki/Folk_Songs_from_Somerset" class="mw-redirect" title="Folk Songs from Somerset">Folk Songs from Somerset</a>\', which provided themes for his <i><a href="/wiki/English_Folk_Song_Suite" title="English Folk Song Suite">English Folk Song Suite</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-150" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-150">&#91;150&#93;</a></sup> In the second folk revival the most famous West country musicians were melodeon-player Bob Cann and writer, performer and broadcaster Cyril Tawney, \'The Father of the West Country Folk Revival\'.<sup id="cite_ref-151" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-151">&#91;151&#93;</a></sup> In the 1970s there were figures such as Tony Rose.<sup id="cite_ref-152" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-152">&#91;152&#93;</a></sup> The same period saw one of the most surprising hybrids in music history <a href="/wiki/Scrumpy_and_Western" title="Scrumpy and Western">Scrumpy and Western</a> with bands like <a href="/wiki/The_Wurzels" title="The Wurzels">the Wurzels</a> and <a href="/wiki/The_Yetties" title="The Yetties">The Yetties</a>, who took most of the elements of West Country folk music for comical folk-style songs with affectionate parodies of more mainstream musical genres, delivered in local <a href="/wiki/West_Country_dialects" class="mw-redirect" title="West Country dialects">West Country dialects</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-153" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-153">&#91;153&#93;</a></sup> More seriously, the West Country and particularly Devon, have produced some of the most successful folk artists of recent years, including <a href="/wiki/Show_of_Hands" title="Show of Hands">Show of Hands</a>, Mark Bazeley and Jason Rice, Paul Downes, <a href="/wiki/Jim_Causley" title="Jim Causley">Jim Causley</a>, <a href="/wiki/Seth_Lakeman" title="Seth Lakeman">Seth Lakeman</a> and his brothers.<sup id="cite_ref-154" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-154">&#91;154&#93;</a></sup> The region has numerous folk clubs and annual festivals, including those at Portsmouth and the <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=rU4MWaJ23og">first modern English folk festival </a> to be established at <a href="/wiki/Sidmouth_folk_festival" class="mw-redirect" title="Sidmouth folk festival">Sidmouth</a> in Devon along with its associated \'Late Night Extra\' venue at <a href="/wiki/Bulverton" title="Bulverton">Bulverton</a> .<sup id="cite_ref-155" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-155">&#91;155&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Yorkshire">Yorkshire</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Folk_music_of_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Yorkshire">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Yorkshire" title="Yorkshire">Yorkshire</a> has a rich heritage of folk music and folk dance including the <a href="/wiki/Long_Sword_dance" title="Long Sword dance">Long Sword dance</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-156" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-156">&#91;156&#93;</a></sup> Folk songs were collected there from the 19th century but, though it probably had more attention than other northern counties, its rich heritage of industrial folk song was relatively neglected.<sup id="cite_ref-157" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-157">&#91;157&#93;</a></sup> It was not until the second revival in the 1950s that Nigel and Mary Hudleston began to attempt to redress the balance, collecting Yorkshire songs between 1958 and 1978.<sup id="cite_ref-158" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-158">&#91;158&#93;</a></sup> Yorkshire folk song lacked the unique instrumental features of folk in areas like Northumbria and was chiefly distinguished by the use of dialect, particularly in the West Riding and exemplified by the song \xe2\x80\x98<a href="/wiki/On_Ilkla_Moor_Baht_%27at" title="On Ilkla Moor Baht &#39;at">On Ilkla Moor Baht \'at</a>\xe2\x80\x99, probably written in the later 19th century and using a Kent folk tune (almost certainly  borrowed via a <a href="/wiki/Methodist" class="mw-redirect" title="Methodist">Methodist</a> <a href="/wiki/Hymnal" title="Hymnal">hymnal</a>), but often seen as an unofficial Yorkshire anthem.<sup id="cite_ref-159" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-159">&#91;159&#93;</a></sup> Most Yorkshire folk songs were not unique and tended to be adapted to fit local geography and dialect, as was the case with probably the most commercially successful Yorkshire song, \xe2\x80\x98<a href="/wiki/Scarborough_Fair_(ballad)" title="Scarborough Fair (ballad)">Scarborough Fair</a>\xe2\x80\x99, recorded by <a href="/wiki/Simon_%26_Garfunkel" title="Simon &amp; Garfunkel">Simon &amp; Garfunkel</a>, which was a version of the Scottish ballad \xe2\x80\x98<a href="/wiki/The_Elfin_Knight" title="The Elfin Knight">The Elfin Knight</a>\xe2\x80\x99.<sup id="cite_ref-160" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-160">&#91;160&#93;</a></sup> The most famous folk performers from the county are <a href="/wiki/The_Watersons" title="The Watersons">the Watersons</a> from <a href="/wiki/Kingston_upon_Hull" title="Kingston upon Hull">Hull</a>, who began recording Yorkshire versions of folk songs from 1965.<sup id="cite_ref-Nidel2005_161-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nidel2005-161">&#91;161&#93;</a></sup> Other Yorkshire folk musicians include Heather Wood (born 1945) of the <a href="/wiki/Young_Tradition" class="mw-redirect" title="Young Tradition">Young Tradition</a>, the short-lived folk rock group <a href="/wiki/Mr_Fox" class="mw-redirect" title="Mr Fox">Mr Fox</a> (1970-2), <a href="/wiki/The_Deighton_Family" title="The Deighton Family">The Deighton Family</a>, <a href="/wiki/Julie_Matthews" title="Julie Matthews">Julie Matthews</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kathryn_Roberts" title="Kathryn Roberts">Kathryn Roberts</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Mercury_Prize" title="Mercury Prize">Mercury Prize</a> nominated <a href="/wiki/Kate_Rusby" title="Kate Rusby">Kate Rusby</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Nidel2005_161-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nidel2005-161">&#91;161&#93;</a></sup> Even considering its position as the largest county in England, Yorkshire has a flourishing folk music culture, with over forty <a href="/wiki/Folk_club" title="Folk club">folk clubs</a> and thirty annual <a href="/wiki/Folk_festival" class="mw-redirect" title="Folk festival">folk music festivals</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-162" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-162">&#91;162&#93;</a></sup> In 2007, the Yorkshire Garland Group was formed to make Yorkshire folk songs accessible online and in schools.<sup id="cite_ref-163" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-163">&#91;163&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Folk_music_of_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="reflist columns references-column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 30em; -webkit-column-width: 30em; column-width: Boosey, <i>Fifty Years of Music</i> (1931, Read Books, 2007), p. 161.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-Brocken2003-16"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Brocken2003_16-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Brocken2003_16-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Brocken2003_16-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Brocken2003_16-3"><sup><i><b>d</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Brocken2003_16-4"><sup><i><b>e</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Brocken2003_16-5"><sup><i><b>f</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Brocken2003_16-6"><sup><i><b>g</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Brocken2003_16-7"><sup><i><b>h</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Brocken2003_16-8"><sup><i><b>i</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Brocken2003_16-9"><sup><i><b>j</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">M. Latham, <i>The Cambridge Music Guide</i> (Cambridge: Cambridge University Press, 1990), p. 472.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-Connell2003-18"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Connell2003_18-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Connell2003_18-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">J.
